  2. Tips into the inflationary extreme, where printing runs unmastered
  3. Avoids money creation
  4. Resolves without cost
Answer

Correct: B. Where austerity and default fed each other into depression, or printing ran to Weimar.

A register of twelve figures behind the machinery of cycles — the practitioner who built the economic machine, the theorists of credit, money and the natural rate, the historians of debt, and the policymakers who fought inflation and its opposite.

01

Ray Dalio

American · b. 1949

Founder of Bridgewater Associates, the largest hedge fund in the world, and the author of the framework on which this chapter is built. Dalio’s How the Economic Machine Works and Principles for Navigating Big Debt Crises decompose the economy into productivity growth and two superimposed debt cycles, and his study of dozens of historical deleveragings produced the four-lever analysis and the distinction between beautiful and ugly outcomes. His enduring insistence — that the model locates you in the pattern without giving you dates, and that the recency error is the investor’s deadliest — makes him the rare practitioner who reduced the credit cycle to a machine plain enough to use.

02

Howard Marks

American · b. 1946

Co-founder of Oaktree Capital and the author of the pendulum this chapter uses to locate oneself in the cycle. Marks built a distressed-debt firm on the discipline he preaches: that markets swing perpetually between greed and fear and never rest at the sensible midpoint, that the turning points cannot be timed, and that the practitioner’s job is therefore location rather than prediction. His memos to clients, read across the industry for three decades, and his book Mastering the Market Cycle supplied the chapter’s governing instruction — that we may never know where we are going, but we had better have a good idea where we are.

03

Milton Friedman

American · 1912–2006

The monetarist whose insistence that inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon supplied the intellectual weapon against the 1966–82 regime this chapter describes. Friedman’s monetary history of the United States, written with Anna Schwartz, reinterpreted the Great Depression as a failure of the central bank to prevent the money supply collapsing — a reading that shaped how his successors would respond to 2008. A Nobel laureate and the century’s most effective champion of free markets, he furnished the doctrine that Paul Volcker would apply, and against which the Keynesians and Austrians of this register defined themselves.

04

Paul Volcker

American · 1927–2019

The Federal Reserve chairman who broke the great inflation and, in doing so, ended one secular regime and began another. Appointed in 1979 with prices rising at double digits, Volcker raised interest rates to punishing levels — inducing a severe recession and enormous political fury — until inflation was crushed and the disinflationary bull market of 1982 to 2000 could begin. He is the human embodiment of this chapter’s gravity: the man who restored the price of money to its full weight, at great cost, and proved that a determined central bank can end a monetary regime that markets had come to believe permanent.

05

Robert Solow

American · 1924–2023

The economist who made productivity growth — the first and slowest of Dalio’s three forces — a measurable quantity. Solow’s growth model, which won the 1987 Nobel, established that the long-run rise in living standards comes overwhelmingly from technological progress rather than the mere accumulation of capital, and gave economics the tools to separate the compounding trend from the credit rhythms that breathe around it. His famous quip that the computer age was visible everywhere but in the productivity statistics is the standing caution of this chapter’s first force: the trend that is nearly everything over generations is almost invisible over years.

06

Friedrich Hayek

Austrian–British · 1899–1992

The leading figure of the Austrian school, whose theory of the business cycle located the origin of booms and busts in credit itself — the argument that artificially cheap money misdirects investment into ventures the real economy cannot sustain, so the bust is the necessary correction of the boom’s malinvestment. Hayek’s 1974 Nobel and his broader case against central planning made him the twentieth century’s great counterweight to Keynes. His cycle theory is the chapter’s third intellectual tradition — distinct from the monetarist and the Keynesian — and its sternest warning against the belief that easy credit is ever truly free.

07

Knut Wicksell

Swedish · 1851–1926

The Swedish economist who gave this chapter’s gravity its original theory. Wicksell distinguished the market rate of interest set by lenders from the natural rate at which saving and investment balance, and argued that when the former is held below the latter, credit expands and prices rise cumulatively — the mechanism beneath every subsequent account of monetary booms, including the modern central bank’s. His idea that there is an equilibrium rate the policy rate can be above or below, with consequences that ripple through every asset, is the analytical foundation on which the discount-rate-as-gravity of Section VI ultimately rests.

08

Carmen Reinhart

Cuban–American · b. 1955

Co-author, with Kenneth Rogoff, of This Time Is Different (2009), the empirical history of financial crises across eight centuries and sixty-six countries that gave this chapter’s long debt cycle its evidence. Reinhart and Rogoff demonstrated that banking crises, sovereign defaults and inflationary episodes follow recognisable patterns, and that the title phrase — the belief that the old rules no longer apply — is the recurring delusion at every peak. A Cuban emigré who became one of the most-cited economists alive and later chief economist of the World Bank, she supplied the historical spine to Dalio’s mechanism.

09

Kenneth Rogoff

American · b. 1953

Reinhart’s co-author and a former chief economist of the International Monetary Fund, Rogoff brought to their collaboration the theorist’s grasp of sovereign debt and the practitioner’s view of crises from the Fund’s vantage. His own work on the debt overhang that follows financial crises — the argument that recoveries from credit busts are slow and shallow in a way ordinary recessions are not — is precisely this chapter’s distinction between a short-cycle recession and a long-cycle deleveraging. A chess grandmaster before he was an economist, he is one of the discipline’s clearest thinkers on why the aftermath of debt is its own animal.

10

Mario Draghi

Italian · b. 1947

President of the European Central Bank through the eurozone crisis, and the author of the three words — “whatever it takes” — that halted a sovereign-debt panic in July 2012 without, at first, spending a euro. Draghi is this chapter’s study in the money-creation lever and the beautiful deleveraging: his bond-buying programmes and negative rates held a fracturing currency union together and placed the central bank’s balance sheet beneath European asset prices for a decade. Later Italy’s prime minister, he embodies the modern truth that a central banker’s credibility can itself be the instrument.

11

Edward Chancellor

British

The financial historian whose The Price of Time is a history of interest rates themselves — the gravity of this chapter, traced from ancient usury laws to the negative rates of the 2010s. Chancellor’s argument, that artificially suppressed interest rates distort investment, inflate asset prices and misallocate capital, is the Austrian caution of Section VI applied to the QE era with a historian’s range. His earlier Devil Take the Hindmost chronicled speculation across the centuries. Trained under the value investors and writing with rare independence, he is the register’s sceptic of the era that placed the central bank beneath every price.

12

Richard Koo

Taiwanese–American

The chief economist of the Nomura Research Institute who named the deleveraging animal this chapter distinguishes from ordinary recession. Studying Japan’s lost decades, Koo described the “balance-sheet recession” — the condition in which the private sector, wounded by a collapse in asset prices, stops borrowing and starts repaying debt regardless of how low rates fall, rendering monetary policy nearly powerless and making fiscal support the only remaining lever. His work is the definitive account of why a long-cycle deleveraging behaves so differently from a short-cycle downturn, and why the conductor’s baton falls silent exactly when it is most needed.

Fifty questions drawn directly from the chapter’s content, in the order of its argument. Commit to a letter before expanding the answer.

1The chapter’s opening proposition is that:

  1. Bubbles are a modern phenomenon
  2. Every generation is issued one great mania, and no generation has yet declined the offer
  3. Manias can be prevented by regulation
  4. Only the ignorant are caught in bubbles
Answer

Correct: B. The chapter is the field guide to an offer no generation refuses.

2The five-act anatomy of a mania is credited to:

  1. Isaac Newton
  2. Irving Fisher
  3. John Kenneth Galbraith
  4. Charles Kindleberger, with Hyman Minsky supplying the credit engine
Answer

Correct: D. The same five-act structure beneath every episode from seventeenth-century Amsterdam onward.

3Act one, displacement, is:

  1. A genuine change — a new technology, market, innovation or policy — that creates real profit opportunities and justifies the first leg of rising prices
  2. A speculative frenzy with no basis
  3. The moment of collapse
  4. The central bank’s intervention
Answer

Correct: A. Manias are not built on nothing; they are built on something true, over-extrapolated.

4The engine of act two, the boom, is always:

  1. Government policy
  2. Credit — the something-true attracts leverage, and Minsky’s migration from sound to speculative finance begins
  3. Foreign investment
  4. Retail enthusiasm alone
Answer

Correct: B. The credit engine is the constant across every specimen.

5In act three, euphoria:

  1. Prices fall sharply
  2. Insiders begin buying
  3. Valuation hands over to narrative, price becomes the evidence for the story, and participation widens from professionals to everyone
  4. Credit contracts
Answer

Correct: C. The phase in which there is nothing so disturbing to one’s judgement as watching a friend get rich.

6Act four, distress, is characterised by:

  1. The central bank raising rates
  2. A fresh wave of buying by professionals
  3. The informed beginning to leave — insiders selling, issuance flooding — while prices, running on the greater fool’s arrival, plateau and churn
  4. The displacement being disproved
Answer

Correct: C. The smart money swaps euphoria’s assets for euphoria’s cash.

7Act five, revulsion, arrives when:

  1. Some small event — often trivial in itself — interrupts the ascent the leveraged structure requires, and the crowd that entered over years attempts to exit through marginal liquidity in weeks
  2. A major war breaks out
  3. Regulators intervene decisively
  4. The technology is proven false
Answer

Correct: A. Minsky’s moment arrives, and Chapter One’s doorway of marginal liquidity proves far too narrow.

8In four hundred years of specimens, the chapter states that:

  1. The acts occur in random order
  2. Every mania is unique in structure
  3. No act has ever been skipped, though the acts vary in length — euphoria especially can run for years
  4. The sequence has shortened over time
Answer

Correct: C. The shape is fixed; only the durations vary.

9In the tulip episode of 1636–37, trading was conducted in:

  1. Physical bulbs exchanged on delivery
  2. Shares in tulip-growing companies
  3. Paper claims on bulbs still in the ground — a derivatives market improvised by florists in a tavern-based futures trade
  4. Government-issued tulip bonds
Answer

Correct: C. Leverage-by-instalment, a widening crowd, and an object valued entirely by the next bid.

10The scholarly caveat the chapter attaches to tulip mania is that:

  1. It never happened at all
  2. It caused a depression
  3. The prices were even higher than reported
  4. Modern scholarship — Garber and Goldgar — has substantially deflated the legend: a narrower circle, prices from moralising pamphlets, contracts settled for fractions, and a Dutch economy that sailed on largely unscathed
Answer

Correct: D. Its honest status is parable more than precedent — a true story inflated by three centuries of retelling.

11Why does tulip mania stay in the field guide despite the caveat?

  1. Because the magnitudes are proven
  2. Because the anatomy is genuine even where the magnitudes are folklore
  3. Because it caused the South Sea Bubble
  4. Because no better example exists
Answer

Correct: B. A volume that flags dubious quote attributions must flag its dubious founding myth with the same rigour.

12The South Sea Company’s scheme was, at its core:

  1. A trading monopoly in the Americas
  2. A debt conversion — holders of government annuities induced to swap them for company shares, with the company able to issue fewer shares the higher its price stood
  3. A property venture
  4. A banking innovation
Answer

Correct: B. The promoters had every incentive to drive the price up — including lending against its own shares to buy its own shares.

13The imitator flotations that surrounded the South Sea scheme:

  1. Were all legitimate ventures
  2. Outlasted the South Sea Company
  3. Were banned before they launched
  4. Gave the phenomenon its name — the “bubbles” — selling shares in ventures of escalating absurdity to a public queuing to be included
Answer

Correct: D. Parliament’s response, the Bubble Act, restricted the joint-stock form itself for a century.

14The South Sea price moved, across 1720, from:

  1. £1,000 to £10,000
  2. Around £100 in January to roughly £1,000 by summer, and back to little above its start by December
  3. £50 to £500 and held
  4. £500 to £100 in a single day
Answer

Correct: B. Fortunes, ministries and the public’s trust went with the collapse.

15Isaac Newton’s sequence in the South Sea Bubble was:

  1. Investing early, selling in spring at a profit, then re-entering near the top with a far larger sum and being carried down for a reported loss of some £20,000
  2. Avoiding the scheme entirely
  3. Shorting the company throughout
  4. Buying at the bottom and holding
Answer

Correct: A. Right analysis, early exit, envy, capitulation to the crowd at the worst moment — Chapter Two performed by the author of the Principia.

16Newton’s case, the chapter says, settles permanently the question of:

  1. Whether the South Sea scheme was fraudulent
  2. Whether intelligence is a defence — it is not
  3. Whether he ran the Royal Mint well
  4. Whether bubbles can be timed
Answer

Correct: B. The greatest scientific mind in history was no exception to Chapter Two’s machinery.

17The line “I can calculate the motions of the heavenly bodies, but not the madness of people” is:

  1. Documented in Newton’s own papers
  2. A modern invention
  3. From Kindleberger
  4. Attributed to Newton, but reported second-hand and never found in his papers
Answer

Correct: D. Flagged, in this volume’s discipline, as an attribution that cannot be verified.

18The railways and the 1920s together establish which uncomfortable feature?

  1. That the underlying technology can be everything its promoters claim, and the investors can still be ruined
  2. That bubbles need no real technology
  3. That governments always intervene
  4. That manias are short-lived
Answer

Correct: A. Being right about the future is no protection against the price paid for it.

19In the British railway mania, leverage was embedded in the instrument itself through:

  1. Broker call loans
  2. Government guarantees
  3. Land collateral
  4. Partly-paid shares — so that capital calls for the remaining money arrived into tightening money in 1847
Answer

Correct: D. The crowd, which included the Brontë sisters and Charles Darwin, discovered the cost of a correct vision.

20The railway mania’s outcome, the chapter says, was that:

  1. The railways were never built
  2. The railways got built; the mania’s investors funded a national endowment for everyone but themselves
  3. Investors were fully repaid
  4. The government nationalised the losses
Answer

Correct: B. A correct vision of the future was no protection whatever against the price paid for it.

21The credit engine of the 1920s was:

  1. Partly-paid shares
  2. Bank lending against land
  3. The broker call loan — shares bought on 10 per cent margin, where a 10 per cent fall erased the buyer: leverage as mass-market product
  4. Government annuities
Answer

Correct: C. The flagship RCA multiplied more than a hundredfold over the decade without paying a dividend.

22Irving Fisher’s epitaph for the new-era doctrine, days before the crash, was that stock prices had reached:

  1. “What looks like a permanently high plateau”
  2. “A temporary summit”
  3. “The end of the cycle”
  4. “A dangerous peak”
Answer

Correct: A. Irving Fisher, 16 October 1929 — the crash arrived within the week.

23By July 1932, the Dow had:

  1. Lost 50 per cent and rebounded
  2. Recovered fully
  3. Lost 89 per cent from its peak, and did not regain it for a quarter of a century
  4. Been suspended indefinitely
Answer

Correct: C. The margin structure did the rest — forced selling begetting forced selling at continental scale.

24Galbraith’s epitaph for every boom’s heroes is that:

  1. “The trend is your friend”
  2. “Markets can stay irrational”
  3. “This time it’s different”
  4. “Financial genius is before the fall”
Answer

Correct: D. John Kenneth Galbraith, A Short History of Financial Euphoria, 1990.

25Japan’s credit engine in the 1980s was a loop of perfect reflexivity in which:

  1. Land collateral funded purchases that raised land values that enlarged the collateral
  2. Broker call loans financed equities
  3. Partly-paid shares embedded leverage
  4. The government guaranteed all lending
Answer

Correct: A. Equities and property inflated together, with corporations earning more from “zaitech” financial speculation than from products.

26The Nikkei peaked on 29 December 1989 at:

  1. 10,000, at modest valuations
  2. 38,915.87, at price–earnings multiples several times the developed-world norm
  3. 50,000, at fair value
  4. Its long-run average
Answer

Correct: B. Contemporary estimates — the era’s favourite illustration — priced the grounds of the Imperial Palace above the entire state of California.

27Japan’s bubbles deflated, after the Bank of Japan raised rates, not in a crash but in:

  1. A single day
  2. A swift two-year recovery
  3. A twenty-year exhalation — the Nikkei halving by 1992, bottoming below 8,000 in 2003, down some 80 per cent
  4. A decade of flat prices
Answer

Correct: C. The banking system’s buried losses produced the “lost decades” of zombie credit and deflation.

28Japan became, the chapter notes, the unacknowledged pilot programme for:

  1. Financial deregulation
  2. The zero-rate, QE world everyone else entered after 2008
  3. High-frequency trading
  4. Cryptocurrency
Answer

Correct: B. The West’s standing cautionary exhibit became its preview.

29The coda to the Japanese specimen, arriving in living memory, is that the Nikkei:

  1. Finally surpassed its 1989 peak in February 2024, thirty-four years later — the longest “stocks always come back” on record
  2. Never recovered
  3. Recovered by 2003
  4. Was replaced by a new index
Answer

Correct: A. The market did come back — outside an investing lifetime, the number every long-term investor should carry.

30Reading the common-anatomy table downward, every displacement was:

  1. Entirely fictional
  2. Impossible to identify
  3. A government invention
  4. Substantially true
Answer

Correct: D. The ruin lay in the price, not the premise.

31Every credit engine in the table, the chapter observes:

  1. Was harmless
  2. Was identical across specimens
  3. Embedded the collapse’s mechanics in advance
  4. Was created by regulators
Answer

Correct: C. Instalments, margin, land collateral — each contained the crash pre-installed.

32Every new-era story in the table existed to:

  1. License the suspension of arithmetic
  2. Warn investors
  3. Attract regulators
  4. Describe the technology accurately
Answer

Correct: A. The appearance of a bespoke metric is the anatomy’s most reliable fingerprint.

33Every trigger in the table was, the chapter emphasises:

  1. A catastrophic external shock
  2. Foreseen by all participants
  3. Small against the move it released — because the trigger never mattered; the structure did
  4. A central-bank decision
Answer

Correct: C. The finale’s violence is set by the leverage, not the news.

34Every aftermath in the table ran longer than:

  1. A single year
  2. The participants’ planning horizon — the column the euphoria phase is constitutionally unable to read
  3. The regulators expected
  4. The displacement itself
Answer

Correct: B. Euphoria cannot read the aftermath column.

35Why does hindsight make identifying manias look easy?

  1. Because the specimens are simple
  2. Because regulators announce them
  3. Because the fingerprints are obvious in advance
  4. Because hindsight removes the only hard part: the inside view — from inside, a mania is emotionally indistinguishable from a new era that happens to be true
Answer

Correct: D. The fingerprints must be read mechanically, against one’s own feelings.

36On the working checklist, dangerous leverage is described as both:

  1. Small and shrinking
  2. Fully regulated
  3. Old and familiar
  4. Growing and novel — each era’s dangerous credit hides in a new instrument precisely because the old ones are still feared
Answer

Correct: D. The new instrument is where the leverage hides.

37“Ubiquity”, on the checklist, is the moment:

  1. The asset is delisted
  2. The asset becomes conversation among people who have never previously mentioned an asset
  3. Regulators approve it
  4. Institutions exit
Answer

Correct: B. Alongside sceptics moving from rebutted to ridiculed, and the doctrine of difference stated openly.

38Templeton’s warning names the four most dangerous words in investing as:

  1. “This time it’s different”
  2. “Buy the dip now”
  3. “The trend continues”
  4. “Stocks always recover”
Answer

Correct: A. Sir John Templeton — the doctrine of difference, stated as a caution.

39The honesty the checklist owes its user is that:

  1. The signals never fail
  2. Identification guarantees a profitable trade
  3. The signals appear only at the top
  4. Every signal fires early — the dot-com bubble displayed the full set by 1997 and ran three more years, doubling and doubling again
Answer

Correct: D. Those who shorted on correct diagnosis were carried out long before being vindicated.

40Identification, the chapter concludes, is worth exactly what Chapter Three said location is worth — namely:

  1. Not a trade, but a posture — declining to hold peak-anatomy risk at peak-anatomy prices, and husbanding the liquidity that revulsion will price like oxygen
  2. A precisely timed trade
  3. A guaranteed short
  4. Nothing at all
Answer

Correct: A. A modest yield on so fine a diagnosis — and, on four centuries’ evidence, the entire yield available.

41The author’s report from the floor is that in 1999 the checklist was:

  1. Unknown to practitioners
  2. Complete, printed, and circulating on the very desks that stayed fully invested — the constraint was never information
  3. Suppressed by regulators
  4. Available only in hindsight
Answer

Correct: B. The crowd includes your management, your clients, your benchmark and your bonus.

42The chapter’s memorable formulation of the practitioner’s predicament is that:

  1. The anatomy of a bubble turns out to include the anatomist
  2. The anatomist stands safely outside
  3. Only outsiders can diagnose manias
  4. Diagnosis guarantees escape
Answer

Correct: A. The field guide reads differently in the field.

43The first recurring theme, “manias are built on truths”, holds that:

  1. Every displacement was real, and the ruin lay in the price, not the premise — being right about the future is no defence against overpaying for it
  2. Every displacement was fictional
  3. Bubbles require no real innovation
  4. Only false stories cause manias
Answer

Correct: A. The chapter’s most uncomfortable lesson, restated as a theme.

44“The story does the valuing” identifies which as the anatomy’s most reliable fingerprint?

  1. Rising trading volume
  2. Central-bank tightening
  3. The appearance of a bespoke metric — a new-era doctrine invented to suspend arithmetic
  4. Falling issuance
Answer

Correct: C. When a new metric is invented for the purpose, the numbers have stopped mattering.

45“Triggers are trivial; structures are not” means:

  1. The trigger determines the severity
  2. Structures are irrelevant
  3. No act in the five-act shape has been skipped in four centuries, and the finale’s violence is set by the leverage, not the news
  4. Every trigger is a major event
Answer

Correct: C. The structure, not the spark, sets the scale of the collapse.

46“Diagnosis pays in posture, not timing” holds that:

  1. Diagnosis allows a perfectly timed short
  2. The signals fire years early, the anatomist is inside the anatomy, and the entire available yield is refusing peak risk at peak prices while keeping the liquidity despair will beg for
  3. Diagnosis is worthless
  4. Timing is the only reward
Answer

Correct: B. The fifth theme, and the chapter’s closing counsel.

47The dot-com episode’s row in the anatomy table lists its trigger and aftermath as:

  1. A war, and a mild correction
  2. Regulation, and no lasting damage
  3. A bank failure, and immediate recovery
  4. Rate rises and funding-window closure in March 2000; the NASDAQ down 78 per cent, its peak regained in 2015
Answer

Correct: D. Its full biography is Chapter Six.

48In act four, distress, the “smart money” is described as:

  1. Buying aggressively
  2. Adding leverage
  3. Swapping euphoria’s assets for euphoria’s cash — while prices run on the greater fool’s arrival
  4. Waiting for the displacement
Answer

Correct: C. The informed leave quietly, before revulsion arrives.

49The chapter describes the Japanese specimen as the largest in the collection because it:

  1. Inflated an entire nation’s asset base at once — equities and property together
  2. Lasted the shortest time
  3. Involved only equities
  4. Was confined to Tokyo real estate
Answer

Correct: A. Its displacement was Japan’s own economic miracle, and the “Japan as Number One” narrative.

50The chapter’s harder question, which the specimens pose, is:

  1. Whether bubbles can be identified at all
  2. Whether the technology is genuine
  3. Which regulator should intervene
  4. What identification is worth from inside one, while it is still inflating and everyone you know is getting rich
Answer

Correct: D. Bubbles can be identified by their fingerprints; the difficulty is acting on the diagnosis from within.

A register of thirteen figures behind the anatomy of a bubble — the theorists who mapped the five acts and their credit engine, the impresarios of the great historical specimens, the most instructive casualty in the history of science, and the scholars who deflated the founding myth.

01

Charles Kindleberger

American · 1910–2003

The economic historian whose Manias, Panics and Crashes (1978) is the natural history of financial folly and the frame for this entire chapter. Kindleberger distilled from four centuries of episodes the recurring five-act structure — displacement, boom, euphoria, distress, revulsion — and married it to Minsky’s credit dynamics, producing the field guide practitioners still carry. An MIT professor who had served in the wartime government and helped shape the Marshall Plan, he wrote with a historian’s range and a sceptic’s wit. His observation that nothing so disturbs the judgement as watching a friend get rich is this volume’s definition of the force that drives the euphoric phase.

02

Hyman Minsky

American · 1919–1996

The economist who supplied the engine beneath Kindleberger’s stages. Minsky’s financial instability hypothesis — the migration of borrowing from hedge through speculative to Ponzi finance as a calm period wears on — explains why the boom of act two carries the collapse of act five inside it. Ignored for most of his career, he was rediscovered in 2008 with such force that the tipping point into crisis is now universally called the “Minsky moment.” In this chapter he is the reason every specimen’s credit engine embedded its own destruction: the leverage that fuels the ascent is precisely what makes the descent uncontrollable.

03

Charles Mackay

Scottish · 1814–1889

The journalist and poet whose Extraordinary Popular Delusions and the Madness of Crowds (1841) first gathered the tulip mania, the South Sea Bubble and the Mississippi scheme into a single chronicle of collective folly, and gave the field its founding literature. Mackay’s vivid, moralising retellings fixed these episodes in the popular imagination for a century and a half — and, as this chapter’s tulip caveat acknowledges, are themselves partly responsible for the legends modern scholarship has had to deflate. He remains the indispensable starting point: the writer who taught financial history to see manias as a recurring human type rather than a series of accidents.

04

John Law

Scottish · 1671–1729

The Scottish financier, gambler and convicted duellist who engineered the Mississippi scheme in France — the 1720 twin of the South Sea Bubble and the most complete demonstration of a mania built by design. Law persuaded the French regency to let him fold a trading monopoly, a national bank and the paper currency into a single edifice, driving shares in his Mississippi Company to dizzying heights before the whole structure collapsed and he fled the country ruined. He is the archetype of the bubble impresario: a genuine monetary innovator whose scheme contained, in its reliance on ever-rising share prices to validate the paper beneath it, the perfect reflexive trap.

05

Sir John Blunt

English · 1665–1733

The principal promoter of the South Sea Company’s 1720 scheme and the man who drove its share price from around a hundred pounds to a thousand. A former scrivener of humble origin, Blunt devised the debt-conversion mechanism at the scheme’s heart and the devices that inflated it — subscriptions on instalment, loans to the public against the company’s own shares to buy yet more shares. When the bubble burst he became the chief target of Parliament’s fury, stripped of most of his fortune by a confiscatory inquiry. He is this chapter’s study in the promoter whose every incentive was to drive the price upward, and who used every one.

06

Isaac Newton

English · 1642–1727

The greatest scientific mind in history and, for the purposes of this chapter, its most instructive casualty. Master of the Royal Mint and no naif in money, Newton invested early in the South Sea Company, sold in the spring at a handsome profit, then — watching the price double again as friends grew rich — re-entered near the top with a far larger sum and was carried down for a reported loss of some twenty thousand pounds. The sequence of right analysis, early exit, envy and capitulation at the worst moment is Chapter Two’s machinery performed by the author of the Principia, and settles for this volume whether intelligence is a defence against a mania. It is not.

07

George Hudson

English · 1800–1871

The “Railway King” who dominated the British railway mania of the 1840s, controlling a substantial share of the nation’s track at the frenzy’s peak. A draper turned promoter, Hudson built his empire on the era’s genuine displacement and its dangerous instrument — partly-paid shares — and on dividends quietly paid out of capital rather than profit. When the calls for unpaid capital arrived into tightening money and the accounts were examined, he was disgraced and pursued for his liabilities. He is the impresario of the specimen that proves this chapter’s hardest point: the railways he floated genuinely remade the economy, and enriched everyone except the investors who funded them.

08

Irving Fisher

American · 1867–1947

The most distinguished American economist of his day, author of the exchange equation and the theory of interest, and the man who declared — days before the 1929 crash — that stock prices had reached what looked like a permanently high plateau. Fisher lost his own fortune in the collapse, but redeemed his reputation intellectually with the debt-deflation theory of great depressions: the account of how falling prices increase the real burden of debt, forcing the liquidation that lowers prices further. He is this chapter’s double lesson — that genius is no protection from the euphoric top, and that the mechanics of the aftermath were understood by the very man the mania humiliated.

09

Jesse Livermore

American · 1877–1940

The most celebrated speculator of the early twentieth century and the model for Reminiscences of a Stock Operator. Livermore made and lost several fortunes reading the tape, and reached his zenith in the crash of 1929, when he shorted the collapse and emerged reportedly some hundred million dollars richer as the market fell 89 per cent. His discipline — trading with the trend, cutting losses, respecting the crowd’s power while positioning against its excess — is the counterpoint to Newton’s ruin: proof that the anatomy can, very rarely, be traded from the short side. That he died by his own hand in 1940, his final fortune gone, completes the cautionary arc.

10

John Kenneth Galbraith

Canadian–American · 1908–2006

The economist and essayist whose The Great Crash, 1929 remains the definitive narrative of that mania, and whose A Short History of Financial Euphoria distilled the pattern this chapter tabulates. Galbraith supplied two of the volume’s governing lines — that financial genius is before the fall, and that the span of financial memory is short enough to guarantee the next episode. A Harvard professor, presidential adviser and ambassador who wrote with unmatched wit, he insisted that speculative episodes recur because each generation credits its own cleverness for what is merely a rising market, and forgets the last disaster in time to stage the next.

11

Yasushi Mieno

Japanese · 1924–2012

The governor of the Bank of Japan who set out, deliberately, to burst the largest bubble in the collection. Taking office in December 1989 — the very month the Nikkei peaked — Mieno judged the land and equity mania a threat to Japanese society and raised interest rates sharply to lance it, an intervention widely credited with ending the boom. What followed exceeded any intention: not a crash but a twenty-year deflation, the “lost decades” of zombie banks and falling prices. He is the chapter’s reminder that the trigger which ends a mania is often a policymaker’s conscious choice — and that the aftermath obeys the structure, not the intent.

12

Peter Garber

American

The economist whose reassessment of the early manias supplied the scholarly caveat this chapter owes its reader. In Famous First Bubbles and related work, Garber argued that the tulip, Mississippi and South Sea episodes were far less irrational than legend holds — that much of the tulip trade reflected the genuine economics of a scarce and reproducible luxury, and that the wildest prices attached to a narrow speculative fringe. His revisionism does not dissolve the anatomy, but it disciplines it: a reminder that financial history, like folklore-Keynes quotations, accumulates embellishment, and that the honest analyst must separate the documented from the moralising tale.

13

Anne Goldgar

American

The historian whose archival study Tulipmania (2007) went to the Dutch records and dismantled much of the received legend. Goldgar found that the trade was confined to a smaller, interconnected community than the myth suggests, that ruinous bankruptcies were rare, that many contracts were quietly renegotiated or ignored, and that the Dutch economy emerged essentially unharmed. Her work, with Garber’s, is why this chapter files tulip mania as parable rather than precedent — the anatomy genuine, the magnitudes folklore. She stands for the discipline the whole volume aspires to: checking the story against the sources, however beloved the story.

Fifty questions drawn directly from the chapter’s content, in the order of its argument. Commit to a letter before expanding the answer.

1The two crashes that open the case histories are chosen because they bracket:

  1. The modern era’s two opposite failure modes — the slow, value-grinding kind and the fast, machine-driven kind
  2. The largest and smallest falls on record
  3. The most and least regulated markets
  4. The American and British markets
Answer

Correct: A. Every later chapter is a variation on one or the other.

2The Bretton Woods system, before 1971, had fixed:

  1. Equity valuations
  2. Interest rates across the world
  3. The oil price
  4. The currencies to the dollar and the dollar to gold — an anchor for prices, trade and expectations
Answer

Correct: D. In August 1971 the United States, bleeding gold, suspended convertibility.

3Once the anchor was gone and currencies floated, money became, for the first time in living memory:

  1. Backed by silver
  2. Purely a promise — and the decade would test what promises were worth
  3. Fixed to oil
  4. Irrelevant to markets
Answer

Correct: B. The anchor for prices, trade and expectations had slipped.

4Britain’s own fire was lit by the 1971 liberalisation of bank credit (Competition and Credit Control) and:

  1. A tax on savings
  2. The expansionary “Barber boom” — sending lending surging, much of it through lightly regulated secondary banks into commercial property
  3. The nationalisation of the banks
  4. Entry into the euro
Answer

Correct: B. Commercial property was the era’s can’t-lose asset.

5The FT 30 index touched its all-time peak in May 1972 at:

  1. 543
  2. 146
  3. 2,722
  4. 1,000
Answer

Correct: A. Equities boiled as credit migrated down Minsky’s quality ladder on schedule.

6The displacement “in the wrong direction” that the structure awaited arrived in October 1973 as:

  1. The Yom Kippur War and the Arab oil embargo, which roughly quadrupled the oil price within months — from around three dollars a barrel toward twelve
  2. A stock-market crash
  3. A currency devaluation
  4. A banking collapse in America
Answer

Correct: A. The largest displacement of the century, in the wrong direction.

7For energy-importing economies, the oil shock delivered the 1970s’ signature condition:

  1. Deflation
  2. Currency appreciation
  3. Rapid growth
  4. Stagflation — costs surging while output fell, the combination that punishes every conventional asset at once
Answer

Correct: D. Inflation destroys bonds while recession destroys earnings.

8Britain’s version of the crisis came “with the lights literally out” because:

  1. The miners’ overtime ban struck coal-fired power just as oil turned scarce — and from 1 January 1974 industry was rationed to a three-day working week
  2. A cyberattack struck the grid
  3. A hurricane hit London
  4. The government banned electricity exports
Answer

Correct: A. Television closed down early by decree, and the government fell in a February election.

9The February 1974 British election was fought on the question:

  1. “In or out of Europe?”
  2. “Who governs Britain?” — producing a hung parliament and a second election within the year
  3. “Boom or bust?”
  4. “Gold or paper?”
Answer

Correct: B. Inflation accelerated toward its eventual peak above 24 per cent in 1975.

10American corporate earnings in the period were, the chapter says:

  1. Growing strongly
  2. Taxed on inflationary paper profits and confiscated in real terms, with dividend controls capping what could be paid
  3. Protected by regulation
  4. Unaffected by inflation
Answer

Correct: B. Equity investors were asked to price businesses whose costs, revenues and currency were all unmoored at once.

11The secondary banks’ fatal structure was that they:

  1. Held only government bonds
  2. Had no leverage
  3. Lent only to households
  4. Funded long, illiquid property loans with short wholesale money — Minsky’s speculative finance in institutional form
Answer

Correct: D. When money tightened and property turned in late 1973, the wholesale market withdrew.

12The failure that began the run, in November 1973, was:

  1. Burmah Oil
  2. London & County Securities
  3. National Westminster
  4. Barings
Answer

Correct: B. The run then moved down the list of fringe names.

13The Bank of England’s “Lifeboat”, convened in December 1973, was:

  1. A nationalisation of the secondary banks
  2. A support operation of the big clearing banks — collective funding lines to solvent-but-illiquid secondary banks, keeping the fringe’s collapse from reaching the core
  3. A ban on property lending
  4. A tax rescue package
Answer

Correct: B. Bagehot’s dictum performed by committee, with support that at its peak exceeded a billion pounds of the era’s money.

14The strain reached “astonishing places” when, by December 1974:

  1. The Bank of England itself failed
  2. The stock exchange closed permanently
  3. Rumours obliged National Westminster — one of the four great clearers — to publicly deny it was in difficulty, a statement whose necessity was itself the news
  4. Sterling was abolished
Answer

Correct: C. In the same month the Bank rescued Burmah Oil, a blue-chip name broken by currency borrowings.

15The Lifeboat’s lesson — that a central bank lending freely against the panic, early, keeps a fringe fire from the main structure — would be remembered in 2008 mostly for:

  1. The cost of initially forgetting it
  2. Being followed exactly
  3. Being irrelevant
  4. Causing the crisis
Answer

Correct: A. The crisis burned two more years but never breached the core system.

16The FT 30 touched its bottom in the first days of January 1975 at 146 — a fall from the 1972 peak of:

  1. Some 48 per cent
  2. Some 73 per cent — a deeper fall than Wall Street’s in 1929–32 when measured in real terms against 24 per cent inflation
  3. Some 22 per cent
  4. Some 89 per cent
Answer

Correct: B. Wall Street’s own bear, January 1973 to October 1974, took the S&P 500 down 48 per cent.

17The prices at the London bottom — the chapter’s “treasure” — were:

  1. Fair value by any measure
  2. Twenty times earnings
  3. Roughly four times earnings, with dividend yields in double digits — blue-chip Britain priced for something close to the end of the world
  4. Higher than the 1972 peak
Answer

Correct: C. Serious commentary discussed whether the system itself would survive.

18By the bottom, the selling had stopped being analysis and become:

  1. Speculation
  2. Arbitrage
  3. Liquidation — institutions raising cash because cash was the only argument left
  4. Hedging
Answer

Correct: C. Disbelief had given way to something quieter.

19The Rothschild line “the time to buy is when there’s blood in the streets” is flagged by the chapter as:

  1. Nineteenth-century folklore in its wording, though the sentiment is 1974’s epitaph
  2. A verified quotation
  3. A line from Greenspan
  4. Recent invention
Answer

Correct: A. The wording is folklore; the sentiment fits the moment exactly.

20In January 1975, against dreadful news, the institutions collectively:

  1. Stopped selling and began, tentatively, to buy — and the market did not recover, it detonated
  2. Sold the remainder of their holdings
  3. Withdrew from equities for a decade
  4. Waited for good news before acting
Answer

Correct: A. The FT 30 roughly doubled in weeks and finished 1975 up well over 100 per cent.

21The 1975 rise was, the chapter notes:

  1. A modest recovery
  2. The greatest calendar-year rise in the FT 30’s history — from a starting point at which almost nobody was invested and almost nobody could bear to be
  3. Driven by good economic news
  4. Confined to a few shares
Answer

Correct: B. The bottom was made not by optimism but by exhaustion.

221974’s twin lessons, the chapter says, have never needed updating — the first being that:

  1. Central banks always rescue the market
  2. Inflation is always temporary
  3. Value bottoms are easy to spot
  4. The point of maximum opportunity is constructed, precisely, out of everything that makes buying feel impossible
Answer

Correct: D. Opportunity is built from the very conditions that make acting on it unbearable.

23The second of 1974’s twin lessons is that:

  1. No bell rang — the bell never rings
  2. The market always warns before turning
  3. Institutions always time the bottom
  4. Recoveries are always gradual
Answer

Correct: A. There is no signal to mark the turn; it is visible only afterward.

24The 1987 bull market, born in August 1982, had by its peak of 2,722 on 25 August 1987:

  1. Run five years, with 1987 alone adding some 40 per cent — valuations at two-decade highs and long bond yields grinding above 10 per cent
  2. Fallen for five years
  3. Doubled in a single month
  4. Traded flat
Answer

Correct: A. Gravity was strengthening beneath a levitating market.

25Portfolio insurance was a computerised strategy that replicated a protective put option by:

  1. Buying put options directly
  2. Selling equity index futures mechanically as the market fell, and buying them back as it rose — downside protection without paying an option premium
  3. Holding cash reserves
  4. Diversifying across countries
Answer

Correct: B. Adopted by pension funds and managers in tens of billions of dollars.

26For any single user portfolio insurance was sound engineering, but in aggregate it was:

  1. A doomsday device on a timer — its core instruction being “the lower the market goes, the more you must sell”: pre-programmed, pro-cyclical, and reflexivity rendered in code
  2. Entirely harmless
  3. A guarantee of profits
  4. Irrelevant to prices
Answer

Correct: A. The sellers of last week’s decline were contractually the sellers of next week’s, in size that grew with the decline.

27The channel that ensured the selling pressure would transmit from Chicago to New York was:

  1. The clearing houses
  2. The foreign-exchange market
  3. The bond market
  4. The arbitrage channel linking the Chicago futures the insurers sold to the New York stocks everyone owned
Answer

Correct: D. The machine sat assembled through the summer, waiting only for a push.

28By the close of Friday 16 October 1987, the week’s fall had:

  1. Reversed entirely
  2. Been contained to 2 per cent
  3. Reached some 10 per cent — enough to arm every portfolio-insurance model with an enormous backlog of selling for Monday
  4. Triggered a central-bank rescue
Answer

Correct: C. The push arrived as an ordinary bad week: poor trade figures, a takeover tax proposal, rate fears.

29The “strangest footnote in crash history” the chapter describes is:

  1. The Great Storm of 16 October 1987 — the worst to strike southern England in centuries — which felled fifteen million trees and shut London down
  2. A computer failure at the exchange
  3. A power cut in New York
  4. A false rumour of war
Answer

Correct: A. Positions went unhedged and orders unworked into a weekend of gathering fear.

30When Monday opened, London faced a special difficulty because:

  1. The storm meant the City had barely functioned on Friday, so London had two days of terror to price at once
  2. The exchange had changed its rules
  3. Trading had been banned over the weekend
  4. Sterling had been devalued
Answer

Correct: A. The wave then ran west with the sun.

31On Monday 19 October 1987, New York delivered the worst single day in the history of modern markets:

  1. The Dow down 89 per cent
  2. The Dow down 10 per cent
  3. The Dow down 508 points, 22.6 per cent, to 1,738.74
  4. The Dow unchanged
Answer

Correct: C. Hong Kong collapsed at the open; London fell 10.8 per cent, then 12.2 per cent more on the Tuesday.

32The mechanics of the Monday were Chapter One’s nightmare rendered exactly — including that:

  1. Buyers flooded the market
  2. Specialists absorbed all the selling
  3. Trading was orderly throughout
  4. Futures crashed to unprecedented discounts beneath the cash market, and for long stretches the largest stocks in the world simply had no bid
Answer

Correct: D. Specialists’ capital was overrun within hours.

33A fifth of American equity value went in a day on no news that could survive the question:

  1. “What will earnings be?”
  2. “Who governs Britain?”
  3. “Compared to Friday, what changed?” — nothing had changed; the structure had simply executed
  4. “Where is the bottom?”
Answer

Correct: C. The collapse was structural, not a repricing of information.

34In the table of worst one-day Dow falls, 19 October 1987 is:

  1. First among equals
  2. Smaller than March 2020
  3. Roughly equal to the 1929 falls
  4. Nearly twice the size of anything else in the record — the signature of a structural failure rather than a repricing of news
Answer

Correct: D. At 22.6 per cent, it stands apart from every other entry.

35The turn on Tuesday 20 October came from:

  1. A large private buyer
  2. A single sentence issued before the New York open by the Federal Reserve, ten weeks into Alan Greenspan’s chairmanship, affirming its readiness to serve as a source of liquidity
  3. A change in the trading rules
  4. Foreign central-bank intervention
Answer

Correct: B. Behind the sentence, the discount window stood open and banks were leant upon to fund the dealers.

36The aftermath delivered the chapter’s second great lesson “by omission” — namely that:

  1. A depression followed
  2. Inflation surged
  3. The market never recovered
  4. No recession came — growth continued through 1988, earnings grew, and the Dow regained its peak within two years
Answer

Correct: D. A recovery as swift as 1974’s was long delayed, because nothing real had broken.

37The principal legacy of the official post-mortem — the Brady Commission — that trades to this day is:

  1. A ban on futures
  2. A tax on portfolio insurance
  3. The circuit breakers that halt the market after preset falls — an admission that the machine can crash itself and must sometimes be forcibly interrupted
  4. The abolition of specialists
Answer

Correct: C. Written into the rulebook as a response to structural failure.

38Samuelson’s joke, promoted by 1987 to analysis, is that:

  1. “The market can stay irrational longer than you can stay solvent”
  2. “This time it’s different”
  3. “Financial genius is before the fall”
  4. “The stock market has predicted nine of the last five recessions”
Answer

Correct: D. Paul Samuelson, 1966 — a load-bearing truth about the market and the economy.

39The first recurring theme is that the two failure modes are distinct:

  1. Both required central-bank rescue
  2. Both were caused by inflation
  3. 1974 was value ground down by real economics; 1987 was structure executing on itself with the economics fine — and the treatment for one is poison for the other
  4. Both were machine crashes
Answer

Correct: C. Value bottoms and machine crashes demand opposite responses.

40“Bottoms are made of exhaustion” is illustrated by:

  1. The Japanese bubble
  2. The 1987 recovery
  3. The dot-com peak
  4. London at four times earnings rising 150 per cent against terrible news, because the last seller had sold
Answer

Correct: D. Opportunity is constructed from everything that makes buying unbearable.

41“Safety strategies aggregate into danger” identifies portfolio insurance as:

  1. A one-off error
  2. A fraud
  3. Individually rational and collectively a doomsday machine — the 1987 edition of a pattern that will recur in 2008’s hedging and 2022’s LDI
  4. A regulatory success
Answer

Correct: C. The same pattern reappears in later chapters wearing different clothes.

42“The lender of last resort is a sentence and a window” refers to the fact that:

  1. Central banks never intervene
  2. Only fiscal policy can end a panic
  3. Rescues always fail
  4. The Lifeboat and the Greenspan statement both worked by removing the question of survival before it could be asked twice
Answer

Correct: D. A credible commitment, backed by an open window, can halt a panic.

43“The market is not the economy” is supported by the observation that:

  1. Crashes always cause recessions
  2. The market always mirrors growth
  3. 1987’s 22.6 per cent predicted nothing, and 1975’s doubling began amid catastrophe
  4. Bottoms coincide with good news
Answer

Correct: C. Confusing the tape for the world is expensive in both directions.

44The macro irritants around the 1987 peak — twin deficits, a strained dollar accord, sharpening words between Washington and Bonn — are described as:

  1. The direct cause of the crash
  2. Real but ordinary — none of it explains what happened
  3. Entirely fictional
  4. A central-bank fabrication
Answer

Correct: B. What explains the crash had been sold to the institutions as safety.

45The author’s report from the floor is that when he arrived in 1995, 1987 was:

  1. Long forgotten
  2. Considered irrelevant
  3. The history the veterans measured everything against — their risk instincts carrying its fingerprints
  4. Blamed on the Great Storm
Answer

Correct: C. A distrust of any strategy promising safety through selling into weakness.

46The question the 1987 veterans taught the author to ask of every elegant hedge was:

  1. “What is the expected return?”
  2. “And who is on the other side when we all want it at once?”
  3. “What does the model say?”
  4. “Is it approved by the regulator?”
Answer

Correct: B. He came to see that this question is the entire education.

47The chapter notes that a new generation’s version of portfolio insurance failed the same test again in:

  1. 2008, as subprime hedging
  2. 2010, as the flash crash
  3. 2000, as the dot-com bust
  4. 2022 — this time wearing a pension scheme’s clothes
Answer

Correct: D. The liability-driven investment episode is foreshadowed here.

48The 1973–74 catastrophe is described as “the ending of a world” principally because:

  1. A new stock exchange opened
  2. The post-war monetary anchor — Bretton Woods — had slipped, leaving floating money, property-collateral lending and an exuberant market awaiting a displacement
  3. Computers replaced traders
  4. The oil price fell
Answer

Correct: B. The stage had to be set because it was the ending of a world.

49The chapter characterises the London 1974 bottom, in real terms, as:

  1. Milder than Wall Street’s 1929–32 fall
  2. Identical to the 1987 fall
  3. A deeper fall than Wall Street’s in 1929–32 when measured against 24 per cent inflation
  4. The mildest bear market on record
Answer

Correct: C. Blue-chip Britain was priced for something close to the end of the world.

50The Tuesday morning after the crash is described as:

  1. The closest the modern system had come to not opening — clearing strained, banks hesitating over dealers’ credit lines
  2. An orderly recovery
  3. A quiet trading day
  4. The start of a recession
Answer

Correct: A. Corporations then announced buybacks into the void, and by Thursday the system had held.

A register of ten figures behind the two bracketing crashes — those who unmoored the money and lit the credit of the 1974 world, the central banker who contained its fire, and the theorists and officials of the 1987 machine.

01

Richard Nixon

American · 1913–1994

The president who ended the post-war monetary order. In August 1971, with the United States bleeding gold, Nixon suspended the dollar’s convertibility — the “Nixon shock” — and within two years the Bretton Woods system of fixed exchange rates had collapsed and the major currencies floated. This chapter’s opening condition, money as purely a promise, is his doing: the anchor for prices, trade and expectations that had held for a quarter-century was cut. His presidency then supplied the American backdrop to 1974 — Watergate, and a resignation — even as the monetary world he had unmoored delivered the decade its inflation.

02

Ahmed Zaki Yamani

Saudi · 1930–2021

Saudi Arabia’s oil minister for a quarter of a century and the public face of OPEC through the 1973 embargo that supplied this chapter’s great displacement. Following the Yom Kippur War, the Arab producers cut output and embargoed supporters of Israel, and the oil price roughly quadrupled within months — the simultaneous tax and supply shock that gave the 1970s their stagflation. Suave, cautious and long the most recognisable figure in energy, Yamani understood the price weapon’s double edge and later warned against pushing it too far. He personifies the external shock that struck a structure already primed by floating money and property-collateral lending.

03

Edward Heath

British · 1916–2005

The Conservative prime minister whose government presided over the British crisis of 1973–74. Confronted by the oil shock and a miners’ overtime ban that struck coal-fired power, Heath imposed the three-day working week from January 1974 and called a February election on the question “who governs Britain?” — and lost his majority, producing a hung parliament and a second election within the year. His premiership frames this chapter’s account of a market priced amid literal darkness and constitutional crisis. It was under his chancellor that the credit boom which fed the secondary-banking collapse had been unleashed two years before.

04

Anthony Barber

British · 1920–2005

Heath’s Chancellor of the Exchequer and the author of the “Barber boom” this chapter identifies as Britain’s self-inflicted fire. His 1971 liberalisation of bank credit, Competition and Credit Control, combined with an expansionary budget to send lending surging — much of it, through a fringe of lightly regulated secondary banks, into the commercial property that was the era’s can’t-lose asset. When money tightened and property turned, that lending became the secondary-banking crisis. Barber’s dash for growth is the chapter’s case study in credit migrating down Minsky’s quality ladder on schedule, primed for the displacement that duly arrived.

05

Gordon Richardson

British · 1915–2010

Governor of the Bank of England through the 1970s crisis and the architect of the Lifeboat. When London & County Securities failed in November 1973 and the run moved down the list of fringe names, Richardson convened the big clearing banks into a collective support operation — funding lines to solvent-but-illiquid secondary banks that kept the fringe’s collapse from reaching the core. His rescue was Bagehot’s dictum performed by committee, and its lesson — that a central bank lending freely and early keeps a fringe fire from the main structure — is the one the chapter says 2008 remembered chiefly for the cost of initially forgetting. He steered the system through without breaching it.

06

Jim Slater

British · 1929–2015

The accountant turned financier whose Slater Walker Securities was the most celebrated of the era’s conglomerate-and-banking ventures — and, when the secondary-banking crisis struck, among its most prominent casualties. Slater embodied the boom this chapter describes: an investment star of the early 1970s whose empire, built on rising asset prices and short-term funding, could not survive their reversal, requiring support and eventually collapsing as the property collateral fell. Later a successful investment writer who candidly described himself as a “minus millionaire” at his nadir, he is the register’s human illustration of Minsky’s speculative finance in institutional form, undone precisely as the model predicts.

07

Hayne Leland

American · b. 1941

The Berkeley finance professor who conceived portfolio insurance and, with Mark Rubinstein and John O’Brien, built the firm that sold it to institutions in the billions. Leland’s insight was elegant: a protective put option could be replicated by trading the underlying — selling as the market fell, buying as it rose — giving downside protection without paying an option premium. For any single fund it was sound engineering; in aggregate it was the doomsday device this chapter describes, its instruction to sell more as prices fell rendering reflexivity in code. He is the author of the innovation that turned an ordinary bad week into 19 October 1987.

08

Mark Rubinstein

American · 1944–2019

Leland’s Berkeley colleague and co-founder, a distinguished theorist of options pricing who helped design and market portfolio insurance. Rubinstein brought deep expertise in derivatives to the strategy’s construction, and lived to reflect with unusual candour on its role in the crash — acknowledging that a tool sound for the individual had, through the sheer scale of its collective adoption, helped destabilise the whole. His later career returned to the academic study of options and market crashes. He stands, with Leland, for this chapter’s central lesson: that financial engineering which is rational for each user can assemble, unseen, into a mechanism lethal for all of them together.

09

Nicholas Brady

American · b. 1930

The investment banker and senator who chaired the presidential task force on the 1987 crash, whose report — the Brady Commission — read the mechanics as this chapter does: portfolio insurance and index arbitrage transmitting a cascade the cash market could not absorb. Its most durable recommendation, the circuit breakers that halt trading after preset falls, remains in the rulebook to this day, an admission that the machine can crash itself and must sometimes be forcibly interrupted. Brady went on to serve as US Treasury Secretary, lending his name to the Brady bonds that resolved the developing-world debt crises. He is the official who turned the crash into a permanent structural reform.

10

Robert Prechter

American · b. 1949

The market forecaster who, through his Elliott Wave newsletter, became the most followed technical analyst of the mid-1980s bull market — his readership and influence peaking in the very run-up to 1987. Prechter’s prominence, and the swings in his guidance around the crash, made him a symbol of the era’s appetite for prediction and the perils of it: a caution that even the most celebrated forecaster is captured by the same cycle he charts. He is included here as this chapter’s reminder that the folklore-Keynes solvency line has a human cost — that being early, or briefly right, is indistinguishable at the time from being wrong.

Fifty questions drawn directly from the chapter’s content, in the order of its argument. Commit to a letter before expanding the answer.

1The confession the chapter asks the reader to hold throughout is that:

  1. The author profited from every episode
  2. The events were all foreseeable
  3. None of what follows looked at the time the way it reads now
  4. The decade was uneventful
Answer

Correct: C. Genius, exuberance, corruption — and the bill, itemised.

2Britain’s “defining domestic casualty” of the early-nineties slump was:

  1. Bank nationalisation
  2. Hyperinflation
  3. A stock-market crash
  4. Negative equity — well over a million households owing more than their homes were worth
Answer

Correct: D. A phrase and a scar that decided British attitudes to property for a decade.

3In October 1990, at the worst possible moment, Britain:

  1. Left the European Community
  2. Joined the European Exchange Rate Mechanism at a demanding DM2.95 to the pound — importing Bundesbank rates set for a Germany financing reunification
  3. Abandoned the pound
  4. Cut rates to zero
Answer

Correct: B. Rates set for reunifying Germany, imposed on an economy on its knees.

4The formative lesson the author’s professional generation received before their careers began was that:

  1. Booms end, property falls, and the word “guaranteed” is a marketing term
  2. Markets always rise over time
  3. Central banks prevent all crises
  4. Diversification eliminates risk
Answer

Correct: A. Delivered by the wreckage of the eighties boom.

5By the summer of 1992 the ERM arrangement was untenable because defending the peg meant:

  1. Cutting rates aggressively
  2. Leaving the exchange rate to float
  3. Promising indefinitely to buy sterling that the entire world had an incentive to sell
  4. Raising taxes
Answer

Correct: C. Britain needed rate cuts it could not make and a currency level it could not hold.

6Soros’s Quantum fund, with Stanley Druckenmiller at the controls, read the sterling position as:

  1. A fair-value trade
  2. Too risky to attempt
  3. A one-way bet underwritten by a government — worst case a small carry cost, best case devaluation
  4. A long position in sterling
Answer

Correct: C. They built a short position against sterling reported at some ten billion dollars.

7On Wednesday 16 September 1992, the market treated each rate escalation by the Bank of England as:

  1. Evidence of resolve
  2. Evidence of desperation rather than resolve — correctly — and kept selling
  3. A reason to buy sterling
  4. Irrelevant
Answer

Correct: B. Base rates were raised from 10 to 12 per cent before lunch, with a further rise to 15 per cent announced for the next day.

8The epilogue that makes Black Wednesday the volume’s specimen is that:

  1. Britain rejoined the ERM
  2. The pound collapsed permanently
  3. Freed from the peg, Britain cut rates, the economy lifted, and a fifteen-year expansion began — the day was renamed White Wednesday
  4. Soros was prosecuted
Answer

Correct: C. What felt like national humiliation turned out to be liberation.

9One of Black Wednesday’s three permanent lessons is that a fixed price defended against fundamentals is:

  1. The surest form of stability
  2. Irrelevant to markets
  3. Always successful if rates rise enough
  4. Not stability but a subsidised exit for whoever moves first
Answer

Correct: D. And reflexivity operates on governments too — the defence’s visible cost became the attack’s best evidence.

10In February 1995, Barings — the City’s oldest merchant bank — was destroyed by:

  1. Unsupervised futures positions built in Singapore by a single trader, Nick Leeson, whose losses of £827 million had been concealed in an error account
  2. A sovereign default
  3. A hostile takeover
  4. The Asian financial crisis
Answer

Correct: A. The bank that had survived Napoleon was sold to ING for one pound.

11The Barings failure was, the chapter insists, not exotic mathematics but the elementary sin:

  1. Excessive diversification
  2. The man who traded also settled and reported his own trades — and management chose not to look closely at profits it enjoyed
  3. Insufficient leverage
  4. A pricing-model error
Answer

Correct: B. The most dangerous number on any floor is a profit nobody can quite explain, because nobody hurries to explain it.

12The displacement of the era became audible in August 1995 when Netscape:

  1. Was acquired by Microsoft
  2. A sixteen-month-old company giving its product away, floated at 28 dollars and touched 71 within hours
  3. Filed for bankruptcy
  4. Reported record profits
Answer

Correct: B. The starting gun of the internet era was audible on every floor in the world.

13The macro chorus that assembled behind the internet displacement included:

  1. Inflation defeated, rates falling, and a productivity miracle plausibly under way
  2. Rising inflation and tightening policy
  3. A deep recession
  4. A currency crisis
Answer

Correct: A. The boom had its truth, its story, and now its ticker symbols.

14The most famous warning in market history, the chapter says, was delivered:

  1. Exactly at the top
  2. Three years and several hundred per cent too early — Greenspan’s “irrational exuberance” question of December 1996
  3. After the crash
  4. By a short-seller
Answer

Correct: B. With the NASDAQ around 1,300, the market dipped overnight and then roughly quadrupled over the following three years.

15The “irrational exuberance” episode is the volume’s standing exhibit on the timing gap because:

  1. The warning was ignored by regulators
  2. Correct diagnosis, publicly delivered by the most powerful financial official on earth, was worth less than nothing as a trading signal
  3. The warning was proved wrong
  4. No one heard it
Answer

Correct: B. Greenspan himself never tightened against the exuberance he had named — the “clean up afterwards” doctrine, chosen in real time.

16The Asian crisis of July 1997 began when Thailand’s pegged baht collapsed under:

  1. The ERM logic transplanted to Asia — fixed exchange rates, dollar-borrowing banking systems, property bubbles
  2. A trade war
  3. A commodity shock
  4. A stock-market crash
Answer

Correct: A. The contagion ran through Indonesia, Malaysia and Korea within months.

17On 27 October 1997, as the Asian contagion spread, the Dow fell 554 points — triggering:

  1. A trading holiday
  2. A central-bank collapse
  3. For the first time, the circuit breakers installed after 1987
  4. The end of the bull market
Answer

Correct: C. The American market absorbed it, the Fed stood ready, and the bull resumed within weeks — the machine had learned that dips recover.

18Long-Term Capital Management assembled which talent?

  1. John Meriwether’s bond-arbitrage veterans and two future Nobel laureates — Merton and Scholes, honoured in 1997, mid-flight
  2. Retail day-traders
  3. Government officials
  4. Venture capitalists
Answer

Correct: A. The cleverest institution ever assembled for the purpose of making money.

19LTCM’s convergence strategy was:

  1. Buying and holding equities
  2. Currency speculation
  3. Buy the cheap instrument, short the expensive near-twin, collect the narrowing spread — slivers industrialised by leverage of roughly 25 times
  4. Venture investing
Answer

Correct: C. On some 4.7 billion dollars of capital, a balance sheet past 125 billion, and a derivatives book whose notional value exceeded a trillion.

20The floor’s standing description of convergence arbitrage was:

  1. “Buy low, sell high”
  2. “Picking up nickels in front of a steamroller”
  3. “Catching a falling knife”
  4. “The trend is your friend”
Answer

Correct: B. Author unknown, verdict unanimous.

21On 17 August 1998, Russia’s default on its domestic debt caused:

  1. LTCM’s trades to converge as planned
  2. The world’s risk appetite to invert overnight — and every spread LTCM owned to widen simultaneously
  3. A rally in risk assets
  4. No effect on LTCM
Answer

Correct: B. Every convergence trade depends on normality returning.

22LTCM’s true position, invisible in its models, turned out to be:

  1. A single trade — short panic, everywhere, on 25 times leverage
  2. A well-diversified book
  3. A long equity position
  4. A cash reserve
Answer

Correct: A. Correlations went to one because the common factor was liquidity itself, and the portfolio’s sophistication was the correlation.

23What produced the LTCM rescue on 23 September 1998 was:

  1. Sympathy for the partners
  2. A private buyer
  3. A government bailout of investors
  4. Systemic entanglement — the fund could not be unwound without detonating the very spreads it owned across every dealer in the world
Answer

Correct: D. The Federal Reserve Bank of New York convened fourteen banks to inject 3.65 billion dollars and inherit the book.

24The residue of the LTCM post-mortem was that:

  1. The models had been poorly built
  2. The partners lacked expertise
  3. The strategy was fraudulent
  4. Intelligence had concentrated, not diversified, the risk; leverage had converted a temporary mispricing into a terminal margin call — being early and being wrong made indistinguishable
Answer

Correct: D. The models had never been better; the models were the position.

25LTCM, the chapter concludes, was:

  1. An isolated failure
  2. Unrelated to any other crisis
  3. 1987’s lesson — safety engineering aggregating into danger — restaged with Nobel laureates, and a full dress rehearsal for 2008
  4. A success eventually
Answer

Correct: C. Ten years early and one decimal point smaller.

26The NASDAQ’s gain in 1999 was:

  1. Flat
  2. 39 per cent
  3. 22.6 per cent
  4. 85.6 per cent — the largest annual gain any major index had ever recorded
Answer

Correct: D. The bull’s final act abandoned all pretence of arithmetic.

27The “bespoke metrics” that arrived on schedule included:

  1. “Eyeballs”, “mindshare”, and price-to-revenue multiples for companies without revenue
  2. Price-to-earnings and dividend yield
  3. Debt-to-equity ratios
  4. Return on capital
Answer

Correct: A. Chapter Four’s most reliable fingerprint — a new metric invented to license the suspension of arithmetic.

28The era’s exhibit for the historic issuance flood was:

  1. Netscape’s float
  2. Amazon’s IPO
  3. The AOL–Time Warner merger
  4. theGlobe.com’s 606 per cent opening pop in late 1998
Answer

Correct: D. Hundreds of technology flotations, first-day doublings routine.

29How were the sceptics treated in the melt-up?

  1. They were vindicated immediately
  2. They were proved wrong permanently
  3. They were promoted
  4. They were not rebutted but retired — value managers who refused the party lost their funds for being right too early
Answer

Correct: D. The beauty contest’s career mechanics executing precisely as Chapter Two specifies.

30The NASDAQ peaked on 10 March 2000 at:

  1. 2,722
  2. 1,114
  3. 5,048.62 — on no news of any kind
  4. 1,300
Answer

Correct: C. The top rang no bell; the index simply stopped going up.

31The retrospective markers of the top — visible only in the rear-view mirror — included:

  1. A recession announcement
  2. A Federal Reserve rate rise
  3. A wave of bankruptcies
  4. A sceptical Barron’s cover, the AOL–Time Warner merger, and Cisco passing Microsoft as the most valuable company on earth
Answer

Correct: D. Cisco stood at north of half a trillion dollars.

32Once the ascent stopped, the structure discovered that its entire logic required:

  1. Lower interest rates
  2. The ascent to continue — losses funded by the next financing round, priced off the last print
  3. Government support
  4. More regulation
Answer

Correct: B. The funding window closed, the greater fool failed to arrive, and three years of gravity resumed at once.

33The bust took three years because it proceeded in layers — the first year:

  1. Cleared the fantasies: the pure dot-coms, Pets.com the mascot, expired as the financing window shut; the NASDAQ fell 39 per cent in 2000
  2. Saw the market recover
  3. Cleared the telecoms
  4. Exposed the frauds
Answer

Correct: A. The companies with no revenue went first.

34The second layer, which carried real debt, was:

  1. Retail brokerages
  2. Property developers
  3. Banks
  4. Telecoms — which had borrowed on an epic scale to build fibre capacity for traffic projections that were marketing documents
Answer

Correct: D. They defaulted across 2001–02 in what was then the largest sectoral credit collapse on record.

35Britain’s “perfect tragedy” was Marconi — the renamed GEC — which:

  1. Survived the bust intact
  2. Was acquired by a rival
  3. Spent a legendary cash mountain at the top buying American telecoms-equipment makers, and rode from 1,250p in 2000 to effective wipe-out within two years
  4. Refused to invest during the boom
Answer

Correct: C. A century’s prudence converted into the bill for eighteen months of fashion.

36The NASDAQ found bottom in October 2002 at 1,114 — down:

  1. 78 per cent — the worst destruction of a major developed index since 1929–32
  2. 39 per cent
  3. 22.6 per cent
  4. 50 per cent
Answer

Correct: A. The 11 September attacks fell within the bear, not causing it.

37The unwind’s subtlest lesson is carried by its greatest survivor, Amazon, which:

  1. Went bankrupt
  2. Was acquired by Microsoft
  3. Rose throughout the bust
  4. Fell 94 per cent, from 107 dollars to under six — every element of the bull case eventually proving true, while the 1999 buyer still waited a decade to break even
Answer

Correct: D. Chapter Four’s first theme — ruin lies in the price, not the premise — in a single ticker.

38The railways of the 1840s made the same bequest as the telecoms, which is that:

  1. Investors were repaid in full
  2. The infrastructure was never used
  3. The infrastructure survived its financiers — the fibre laid by bankrupt telecoms became the cheap plumbing of the next era’s giants
  4. The technology failed
Answer

Correct: C. The mania funded a future for everyone except its own investors.

39Galbraith’s framework, waiting for the tide’s retreat, observed that embezzlement uniquely enjoys:

  1. Immunity from prosecution
  2. The approval of regulators
  3. A period in which the thief has the money and the victim feels no loss
  4. Low returns
Answer

Correct: C. At any given time there exists an inventory of undiscovered embezzlement — the “bezzle”.

40The bezzle, in Galbraith’s account, swells in booms and is discovered in busts because:

  1. Regulators change the rules
  2. In booms money is plentiful and nobody checks; in busts everybody does
  3. Thieves confess in downturns
  4. Auditors work harder in booms
Answer

Correct: B. The 2001–02 discoveries were the largest bezzle liquidation in history to that date.

41Enron collapsed in weeks in late 2001 when its structure lost the market’s confidence — a structure of:

  1. Simple over-borrowing
  2. Off-balance-sheet entities hiding debt, and mark-to-model earnings booked on hope — the floor’s coinage, “mark-to-myth”, the precise corruption of Chapter One’s Level 3
  3. Currency speculation
  4. Property lending
Answer

Correct: B. The seventh-largest company in America by revenue, a six-time “most innovative”, produced the largest bankruptcy then recorded.

42The audit firm destroyed by its role in Enron — “Barings’ lesson unlearned at civilisational scale” — was:

  1. One of the five great audit firms, Arthur Andersen
  2. A small regional practice
  3. An internal Enron department
  4. A credit-rating agency
Answer

Correct: A. The auditor that had enjoyed the profits chose not to look closely at them.

43WorldCom, seven months later, was a fraud of brutal simplicity:

  1. Fake overseas subsidiaries
  2. Currency manipulation
  3. Insider trading
  4. Billions of ordinary operating costs booked as capital investment — ultimately some 11 billion dollars of fiction
Answer

Correct: D. It produced a still larger bankruptcy and a 25-year sentence for its chief executive.

44The legislative response of July 2002, Sarbanes–Oxley:

  1. Made executives personally certify their accounts and rebuilt audit independence around a new regulator
  2. Banned short-selling
  3. Abolished mark-to-market accounting
  4. Nationalised the audit firms
Answer

Correct: A. The deeper accounting was Chapter Two’s: none of it was hidden from those minded to look.

45Why were the frauds not caught earlier, in the chapter’s account?

  1. The information was genuinely concealed
