  2. Short sellers and a handful of analysts had published the questions in real time and been vilified — because in the euphoria, looking was the deviant act
  3. Regulators lacked the power
  4. The frauds were too complex to detect
Answer

Correct: B. Rising prices suspend the looking.

46The bezzle, the chapter concludes, is not an anomaly of booms but:

  1. A rare accident
  2. A product line — manufactured when rising prices audit nothing, delivered when falling prices audit everything
  3. A regulatory failure
  4. Confined to America
Answer

Correct: B. The tide’s retreat is the only auditor with subpoena power over everyone at once.

47The first recurring theme, “pegs and promises invite the test”, holds that Black Wednesday and the Asian crisis were:

  1. Entirely different events
  2. Caused by fraud
  3. The same trade — a fixed price defended against fundamentals is a subsidised exit for whoever moves first, and governments can lose
  4. Prevented by central banks
Answer

Correct: C. The ERM logic transplanted to Asia produced the same collapse.

48“Early is indistinguishable from wrong” is illustrated by the fact that:

  1. Greenspan named the exuberance in 1996, the sceptics were retired for agreeing with him, and diagnosis without solvency is a donation to the euphoric
  2. The warning came too late
  3. No one warned at all
  4. The sceptics profited handsomely
Answer

Correct: A. The folklore-Keynes solvency line, collecting another generation of tuition.

49“Intelligence concentrates risk when leverage industrialises it” refers to:

  1. LTCM’s genius building a single hidden position — short panic, everywhere — where the models were the exposure
  2. The dot-com day-traders
  3. Enron’s accounting
  4. The Asian crisis
Answer

Correct: A. Sophistication was the correlation.

50“The premise survives; the price does not” is the lesson that:

  1. The internet was a hoax
  2. Only fraud drives bubbles
  3. Technology never matters to markets
  4. The internet was everything promised, and Amazon’s 94 per cent drawdown priced the difference between being right and being paid
Answer

Correct: D. Being right about the future is no defence against overpaying for it.

A register of sixteen figures behind the decade — the speculators who broke a peg, the trader who broke a bank, the builders and analysts of the internet boom, the Nobel laureates whose genius failed, and the executives whose frauds the bust exposed.

01

Nigel Lawson

British · 1932–2023

Chancellor of the Exchequer through the mid-1980s, whose expansionary “Lawson boom” and its inflationary end set the stage for this chapter’s cold opening. The tax cuts and deregulation of his tenure fed a credit and housing boom whose reckoning — base rates at 15 per cent, houses in outright fall, over a million households in negative equity — defined the early-nineties slump. A committed advocate of a stable exchange rate, he shadowed the Deutsche Mark before Britain formally joined the ERM, the arrangement whose destruction is the chapter’s second act. His career is the reminder that the boom whose bust opens a decade was engineered in the confidence of the one before.

02

Stanley Druckenmiller

American · b. 1953

The trader at the controls of Soros’s Quantum fund during the 1992 assault on sterling, and one of the outstanding macro investors of his generation. It was Druckenmiller who sized the sterling short at some ten billion dollars, reading Britain’s ERM defence as a subsidised exit — and Soros who urged him to “go for the jugular” and enlarge it. Black Wednesday made his reputation; the dot-com years nearly unmade it, when Quantum’s late capitulation to technology stocks cost heavily near the top — a personal illustration of this chapter’s lesson that even the disciplined are drawn into the euphoria. His career spans reflexivity applied and reflexivity suffered.

03

Nick Leeson

British · b. 1967

The trader whose unsupervised futures positions destroyed Barings, the City’s oldest merchant bank, in a fortnight in February 1995. Operating in Singapore with control of both dealing and settlement, Leeson concealed mounting losses in an error account while his reported profits made him a star, until the losses reached £827 million and the bank that had survived Napoleon was sold to ING for one pound. His case is this chapter’s pure specimen of the elementary control failure — the man who traded also settled his own trades, and management declined to scrutinise the profits it enjoyed. Every segregation of duties in the author’s profession was re-armed by his fraud.

04

Marc Andreessen

American · b. 1971

The programmer who co-wrote the first widely used graphical web browser and co-founded Netscape, whose August 1995 flotation — a sixteen-month-old company giving its product away, floated at 28 dollars and touching 71 within hours — fired the starting gun of the internet era. Andreessen is the human face of this chapter’s displacement, Chapter Four’s first act in its purest modern form: a genuine technological revolution that would remake the economy, and simultaneously the occasion for the greatest equity mania in a generation. Later a leading venture capitalist, he embodies the truth that the displacement is always real — the ruin lies in what the market pays for it.

05

Alan Greenspan

American · b. 1926

Chairman of the Federal Reserve from 1987 to 2006, and the presiding figure of this chapter and the next. Greenspan asked the most famous question in market history — how to know when “irrational exuberance” had unduly escalated asset values — in December 1996, three years and several hundred per cent too early, and then declined to act on his own diagnosis, choosing the doctrine of cleaning up after a bubble rather than leaning against it. The “Greenspan put”, the market’s belief that he would always ease into trouble, was forged in his response to 1987 and confirmed by LTCM. Revered as the maestro in his day, he left the dependency that the following chapters inherit.

06

Robert Shiller

American · b. 1946

The Yale economist who gave the era its name and its warning. Shiller’s Irrational Exuberance — published, with immaculate timing, in March 2000 at the very peak — argued that stock prices were driven by contagious narratives and feedback rather than fundamentals, and correctly called the dot-com bubble a bubble. His cyclically adjusted price-earnings measure remains a standard gauge of long-run valuation, and his later work on narrative economics supplies Chapter Two’s account of stories as epidemics. A Nobel laureate in 2013 — shared with the efficient-markets champion Eugene Fama — he is the register’s scholar of the behavioural forces this whole volume treats as primary.

07

John Meriwether

American · b. 1947

The former Salomon Brothers bond-arbitrage chief who founded Long-Term Capital Management and assembled the most formidable quantitative talent ever gathered in one firm. Meriwether’s convergence trades — buying the cheap instrument, shorting its expensive near-twin, collecting the narrowing spread — earned returns above 40 per cent until Russia’s 1998 default turned the leverage lethal and every spread widened at once. His fund’s near-collapse required a Fed-convened rescue by fourteen banks, and stands as the dress rehearsal for 2008. That he launched a successor fund which also foundered in the later crisis completes the chapter’s lesson on how little genius avails against leverage and correlation.

08

Myron Scholes

Canadian–American · b. 1941

Co-author of the Black–Scholes option-pricing formula, Nobel laureate in 1997, and a partner in Long-Term Capital Management at the moment of its unravelling. Scholes brought the intellectual prestige of modern finance to the fund — and, with it, this chapter’s sharpest irony: that the theorists who had taught the world to price and hedge risk were themselves destroyed by the one risk their models excluded, the disappearance of liquidity when every position becomes the same position. His award, received mid-flight in 1997, and his fund’s failure a year later, frame the volume’s recurring warning that the model can become the exposure.

09

Robert Merton

American · b. 1944

The economist who extended option-pricing theory into the continuous-time mathematics that underpins modern derivatives, Nobel laureate alongside Scholes in 1997, and the third of Long-Term Capital Management’s intellectual titans. Merton’s theoretical contributions were foundational to the entire architecture of quantitative risk management the fund embodied — and his presence, with Scholes’s, made LTCM’s collapse a verdict not on ignorance but on the limits of the discipline itself. That the models had never been better, yet the models were the position, is this chapter’s standing paradox, and Merton its most distinguished author. He returned to academia to work on the very financial-stability questions his fund had so vividly posed.

10

Julian Robertson

American · 1932–2022

Founder of Tiger Management and one of the most successful hedge-fund investors of his era, whose fate is this chapter’s cleanest illustration of the sceptic retired for being right too early. A disciplined value investor, Robertson refused to buy the dot-com stocks he judged absurd, underperformed badly as they soared, suffered redemptions, and closed his funds to outside money in March 2000 — within weeks of the exact top. The market vindicated his judgement almost immediately, and far too late for his firm. His “Tiger Cubs” went on to found many of the next generation’s great funds, but his own exit stands as the beauty contest’s career mechanics executed to the letter.

11

Mary Meeker

American · b. 1959

The Morgan Stanley analyst dubbed the “Queen of the Net”, whose research anointed the internet companies the boom would chase and whose bullish reports carried enormous weight with investors. Meeker was among the most influential voices of the era’s optimism, championing the “eyeballs” and market-share metrics that this chapter identifies as bespoke measures invented to license the suspension of arithmetic. Criticised after the bust for the gap between sell-side enthusiasm and outcomes, she rebuilt a distinguished career in venture capital and became known for her exhaustive annual internet-trends reports. She personifies the analyst’s role in the machine — the manufacture of the narrative that does the valuing.

12

Henry Blodget

American · b. 1966

The Merrill Lynch analyst whose spectacular price targets on internet stocks made him a symbol of the era’s sell-side excess. Blodget rose to fame with a bullish call on Amazon that the market briefly exceeded, and his ratings carried the boom’s conviction to retail investors. The bust exposed the conflict beneath the research: internal messages disparaging stocks his published notes promoted led to charges, a settlement, and a lifetime ban from the securities industry. He reinvented himself as a financial journalist and founder of a business-news venture. He stands, with Meeker, for this chapter’s account of how the euphoria’s justifying literature was written, and by whom, and at what eventual cost.

13

Jeff Bezos

American · b. 1964

Founder of Amazon, and the register’s embodiment of the chapter’s subtlest lesson: that the premise can be entirely correct and the price still ruinous. Bezos built, from an online bookseller, one of the greatest businesses in history — every element of the 1999 bull case eventually vindicated. Yet Amazon’s shares fell 94 per cent in the bust, from 107 dollars to under six, and the investor who bought at the euphoric peak waited a decade merely to break even. His company is Chapter Four’s first theme in a single ticker — ruin lies in the price, not the premise — and the proof that surviving a mania and being a good investment in it are entirely different things.

14

Arnold Weinstock

British · 1924–2002

The managing director who built GEC into Britain’s greatest industrial company and husbanded a legendary cash reserve through decades of disciplined caution — the prudence this chapter watches destroyed. After Weinstock’s retirement, his successors renamed the firm Marconi and spent the entire cash mountain at the top of the telecoms boom on American equipment makers, riding the shares from over 1,250 pence to effective wipe-out within two years. His life’s conservatism, converted into the bill for eighteen months of fashion, is the chapter’s perfect British tragedy: a century’s accumulated prudence spent, at the worst possible moment, chasing a narrative. He died as the collapse he had spent his career guarding against consumed his creation.

15

Jeffrey Skilling

American · b. 1953

The chief executive of Enron whose championing of mark-to-market accounting and off-balance-sheet financing built the structure that collapsed in weeks in late 2001. Skilling drove the culture in which future profits were booked today on models built of hope — the floor’s “mark-to-myth”, the precise corruption of Chapter One’s Level 3 — turning America’s seventh-largest company by revenue into its largest bankruptcy then recorded. Convicted of fraud and conspiracy in 2006, he served over a decade in prison. He is this chapter’s central figure in the bezzle — the executive who, in a market that audited nothing while prices rose, manufactured earnings the tide’s retreat exposed as fiction.

16

Bernie Ebbers

American · 1941–2020

The chief executive who built WorldCom into a telecoms giant through relentless acquisition and then presided over a fraud of brutal simplicity: billions of ordinary operating costs booked as capital investment, ultimately some eleven billion dollars of fiction, producing a bankruptcy larger even than Enron’s. Where Enron’s deceptions were baroque, Ebbers’s were crude — a straightforward lie about which expenses were which — and made Enron, as this chapter puts it, look artisanal. Convicted in 2005, he received a twenty-five-year sentence. His collapse, alongside Enron’s, prompted Sarbanes–Oxley and stands as the chapter’s proof that the boom does not interrupt the fraud; it commissions it.

Fifty questions drawn directly from the chapter’s content, in the order of its argument. Commit to a letter before expanding the answer.

1The chapter frames 2008 as the convergence of every earlier lesson — describing it as:

  1. An unprecedented event with no historical parallel
  2. Kindleberger’s anatomy at global scale, Minsky’s migration performed by the entire banking system, and reflexivity running through the world’s housing stock — all at once
  3. A purely American crisis
  4. A failure of regulation alone
Answer

Correct: B. With the payments system itself as the stake.

2The crisis, the chapter says, was assembled from:

  1. A collapse in corporate earnings
  2. A sudden external shock
  3. The previous crisis’s medicine — money at 1 per cent into 2003–04, gravity suspended
  4. A currency crisis
Answer

Correct: C. The Federal Reserve’s answer to the dot-com bust routed a burned public toward housing.

3The asset the public turned to was the one that had:

  1. The highest dividend yield
  2. “Never fallen nationally in living memory” — housing, the exuberance’s new address
  3. Government guarantees
  4. The lowest volatility
Answer

Correct: B. Reflexivity did the rest: rising prices enlarged collateral, enlarged collateral eased credit, eased credit raised prices.

4The global savings glut supplied the demand side by:

  1. Buying equities
  2. Recycling Asian and petro surpluses into any instrument stamped safe — the world wanting AAA paper in quantities genuine AAA borrowers could not supply
  3. Lending directly to households
  4. Purchasing gold
Answer

Correct: B. Wall Street undertook to manufacture the difference.

5The “originate-to-distribute” model worked by:

  1. Lenders holding their mortgages to maturity
  2. Borrowers financing each other
  3. Government purchase of all mortgages
  4. Lenders selling the mortgages they wrote within weeks into securitisation pipelines — severing the connection between the person assessing the credit and the person bearing it
Answer

Correct: D. Volume replaced quality, because volume was what the pipeline paid for.

6The floor’s four-letter summary of the incentive structure was:

  1. “AAA–OK”
  2. “LIBOR–OIS”
  3. “IBG–YBG” — I’ll be gone, you’ll be gone
  4. “ABX–CDO”
Answer

Correct: C. The marginal mortgage was written to a borrower whose ability to pay was, by design, nobody’s problem.

7The “escalation” beyond the legitimate mortgage-backed security was the CDO, which:

  1. Held only government bonds
  2. Was built from the unwanted lower slices of other securitisations, re-tranched so the top of the new stack was again stamped AAA
  3. Reduced leverage in the system
  4. Eliminated default risk
Answer

Correct: B. Then CDOs of CDOs, and synthetic CDOs via credit default swaps — an inverted pyramid resting on the same thin layer of actual subprime borrowers.

8The mathematical assumption enabling the alchemy — the Gaussian copula its emblem — was:

  1. That house prices would rise forever
  2. That the government would guarantee the loans
  3. That interest rates would remain fixed
  4. A bet that defaults would stay uncorrelated — that Nevada would not fail with Florida — priced from decades in which national house prices had not fallen
Answer

Correct: D. One national downturn falsified every copy of the assumption simultaneously.

9The AAA stamp itself had been captured because the rating agencies:

  1. Were government departments
  2. Refused to rate structured products
  3. Were paid by the issuers whose products they graded, in a business where the structurer could shop between agencies and reverse-engineer to their published models
  4. Had no involvement in the boom
Answer

Correct: C. Structured finance had become the agencies’ profit engine.

10Buffett’s warning, written in 2002 and mocked for missing the party, described derivatives as:

  1. “Financial weapons of mass destruction, carrying dangers that, while now latent, are potentially lethal”
  2. “A permanently high plateau”
  3. “Picking up nickels in front of a steamroller”
  4. “A source of liquidity”
Answer

Correct: A. Warren Buffett, Berkshire Hathaway letter, 2002 — describing the structure’s character in advance.

11The great broker-dealers ran balance sheets at:

  1. Two or three times capital
  2. Ten times capital, fully deposit-funded
  3. No leverage at all
  4. Thirty times capital and upward — a structure in which a 3 per cent fall in asset values is extinction, funded overnight in the repo market
Answer

Correct: D. Funded against the very securities in question.

12The “shadow banking system” of SIVs and conduits performed:

  1. Maturity transformation without deposit insurance or a lender of last resort — funding long structured assets with short commercial paper
  2. Deposit-taking with full insurance
  3. Only government lending
  4. Equity underwriting
Answer

Correct: A. The system had rebuilt banking’s eternal fragility outside every framework designed after the last time.

13Chuck Prince’s July 2007 interview supplied the era’s epitaph:

  1. “This time it’s different”
  2. “Financial genius is before the fall”
  3. “As long as the music is playing, you’ve got to get up and dance. We’re still dancing”
  4. “Buy when there’s blood in the streets”
Answer

Correct: C. Read against Chapter Two it is not folly but confession — the beauty contest’s career mathematics, stated by its most senior contestant.

14The crisis proper began, the chapter says, not with a crash but with:

  1. A sentence in a fund notice — BNP Paribas freezing three funds on 9 August 2007, citing the “complete evaporation of liquidity”
  2. A stock-market decline
  3. A central-bank rate rise
  4. A bank nationalisation
Answer

Correct: A. The first official admission that the alphabet had no prices.

15The money markets understood the BNP Paribas notice immediately to mean that:

  1. Only three funds were affected
  2. The Fed would cut rates
  3. If those assets could not be valued, then neither could anyone’s collateral, nor any counterparty — and interbank lending seized that morning
  4. Equities would fall first
Answer

Correct: C. LIBOR–OIS, the system’s pulse, blew out toward levels never recorded.

16The form the crisis kept for fourteen months was:

  1. A run on the shadow banking system, conducted by institutions, in wholesale markets the public could not see
  2. Falling equity prices
  3. A currency collapse
  4. A wave of corporate frauds
Answer

Correct: A. Equity prices held up deceptively into the autumn.

17Northern Rock’s run, made visible on 14 September 2007, was:

  1. The largest bank failure in history
  2. The first run on a British bank since Overend Gurney in 1866 — queues down British high streets after its request for Bank of England support leaked
  3. A purely wholesale event
  4. Contained without public knowledge
Answer

Correct: B. Its funding model — mortgages funded by continuous securitisation — stopped working the week the pipeline froze.

18Mervyn King’s caption for the episode was that:

  1. “The music has stopped”
  2. “Genius has failed”
  3. “Derivatives are weapons of mass destruction”
  4. “It is not rational to start a bank run, but it is rational to participate in one once it has started”
Answer

Correct: D. A blanket deposit guarantee stopped the queues; nationalisation followed in February 2008.

19Bear Stearns fell in March 2008 by way of a “repo run”, meaning:

  1. Its overnight lenders declined to roll and hedge-fund clients fled — the fifth-largest US investment bank going from adequate to finished in roughly seventy-two hours
  2. Depositors queued at branches
  3. It was hit by a fraud
  4. Regulators closed it
Answer

Correct: A. Sold to JPMorgan over a weekend at two dollars a share, with the Fed guaranteeing 29 billion dollars of its book.

20Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac were taken into conservatorship on 7 September 2008 because they were:

  1. Guarantors of half the US mortgage market — the state absorbing that half
  2. Small regional lenders
  3. Foreign banks
  4. Hedge funds
Answer

Correct: A. The summer consumed the housing agencies themselves.

21Over the Lehman weekend of 12–14 September, the last private buyers fell away because:

  1. No bank was interested
  2. The Fed forbade any purchase
  3. Bank of America chose Merrill Lynch instead, and Barclays was willing but blocked by its British regulator from guaranteeing Lehman’s book mid-crisis
  4. Lehman refused to be sold
Answer

Correct: C. The US authorities judged they lacked legal authority to lend into so deep an insolvency, and let it go.

22At 1.45 a.m. on 15 September 2008, Lehman’s filing — the largest bankruptcy in history at some 600 billion dollars — falsified:

  1. The efficient-markets hypothesis
  2. The assumption underneath every market since 1987, that the system’s core would always be caught
  3. The Gaussian copula
  4. Bagehot’s dictum
Answer

Correct: B. Falsified in a single filing.

23AIG was rescued the following day because its London unit had:

  1. Made bad property loans
  2. Run a rogue-trading desk
  3. Sold credit default swaps insuring the alphabet’s AAA tranches to the entire world — systemic risk concentrated in a single seller
  4. Held only equities
Answer

Correct: C. Its failure would detonate every bank it had insured; an 85 billion dollar government line followed.

24When the Reserve Primary Fund “broke the buck”, the run went universal because:

  1. Only that fund was affected
  2. Money funds — the cash accounts of corporate America — faced redemptions that froze the commercial-paper market overnight, until a Treasury guarantee stopped it
  3. Equities collapsed
  4. Banks stopped lending to households
Answer

Correct: B. The run had reached the public’s cash.

25Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley, the last independent investment banks, survived the eight days by:

  1. Merging with each other
  2. Refusing all government help
  3. Being nationalised
  4. Converting to bank holding companies to reach the Fed’s window
Answer

Correct: D. The Wall Street of the previous thirty years ceased to exist in eight days.

26The “crash-within-the-crisis” came on 29 September 2008 when:

  1. Lehman filed
  2. The House of Representatives rejected the 700 billion dollar TARP rescue and the Dow fell 778 points — a real-time referendum on the price of no rescue
  3. AIG was rescued
  4. Northern Rock was nationalised
Answer

Correct: B. At the time the largest points fall ever; the bill passed on 3 October.

27As the crisis went fully global in early October, the chapter notes that:

  1. Only America was affected
  2. Iceland’s banking system failed outright, and RBS — freshly gorged on its catastrophic ABN AMRO acquisition — came within hours of inability to fund
  3. Markets recovered quickly
  4. Asia was the epicentre
Answer

Correct: B. The following week was the worst in the S&P 500’s history to that date, down some 18 per cent in five sessions.

28As the alphabet stopped trading, the fair-value ladder collapsed downward — and a handful of distressed indices came to dominate:

  1. Level 1 expanded
  2. All positions became observable
  3. The ABX, built on a few dozen deals, ended up steering the marks on hundreds of billions, as vast books arrived on Level 3
  4. Marking was suspended entirely
Answer

Correct: C. On Level 3 the mark is an argument, conducted daily, between a desk defending its capital and a control function defending the truth.

29The “doom loop” the chapter describes ran:

  1. Higher marks to higher capital to more lending
  2. Only at a single bank
  3. Independently of the marks
  4. Lower marks to lower capital to forced deleveraging to forced sales into a bidless market, printing the next lower “observable” level — at every institution simultaneously
Answer

Correct: D. Mark-to-market, a truth-telling device in peacetime, had become the crisis’s transmission mechanism.

30By early 2009 the pressure to bend the marking rules was itself systemic, and:

  1. The rules were left unchanged
  2. Marking was abolished
  3. The accounting standard-setters relaxed the rules that April under open political duress
  4. The agencies took over marking
Answer

Correct: C. A moment practitioners still argue about.

31One of the two convictions the author dates from 2008 is that an institution’s survival is decided:

  1. Years earlier, in whether its culture let the marks be honest when honesty was cheap
  2. By luck in the crisis itself
  3. By the size of its balance sheet
  4. By its regulator
Answer

Correct: A. The controller is not auditing the crisis — the controller is in it, because the marks decide who lives.

32The other conviction is that the phrase “it’s only a liquidity problem” is:

  1. Always true
  2. A regulatory term
  3. Never relevant in a crisis
  4. What insolvency says in public — sometimes truthfully, which is exactly the difficulty Bagehot left us
Answer

Correct: D. Distinguishing illiquidity from insolvency, under deadline, is the lender of last resort’s central problem.

33What ended the run was the arrival of:

  1. Good economic news
  2. A private consortium
  3. The only balance sheet that cannot be run upon — the state, on every front at once
  4. A recovery in house prices
Answer

Correct: C. Britain’s 8 October package set the template the world copied within days.

34Britain’s 8 October package differed from mere liquidity support because it involved:

  1. Only interest-rate cuts
  2. Recapitalisation — the state buying equity in its banks, ultimately taking majority ownership of RBS — alongside funding guarantees
  3. Nationalising the whole banking system
  4. A tax rebate
Answer

Correct: B. The same day, six central banks cut rates in the first coordinated move in history.

35The Federal Reserve built, facility by facility, a “Bagehot machine of unprecedented scope” that lent:

  1. Only to the largest banks
  2. Nothing at all
  3. Only to the US government
  4. Freely against widening collateral to banks, dealers, money funds and the commercial-paper market, with dollar swap lines to the world’s central banks
Answer

Correct: D. The Lifeboat and the 1987 statement re-run at two orders of magnitude greater.

36The rescue, the chapter is careful to say:

  1. Did not stop the bear market — it stopped the run; prices kept falling for five more months as the recession fed back into earnings
  2. Stopped the bear market immediately
  3. Caused the crash
  4. Had no effect
Answer

Correct: A. Stopping a run and stopping a bear market are different achievements.

37With the conventional lever exhausted at zero rates, the long-cycle machinery engaged:

  1. Austerity
  2. Redistribution
  3. Default and restructuring
  4. Quantitative easing — the money-printing lever of Dalio’s four, pulled in size, the beginning of the era Chapter Eight inhabits
Answer

Correct: D. The Fed from November 2008 and at scale from March 2009, the Bank of England from March 2009.

38The equity bottom came on 9 March 2009, with the S&P 500 at 676.53 — down:

  1. 57 per cent from the October 2007 peak of 1,565, with the VIX having set its record closing high of 80.86 the previous November
  2. 18 per cent
  3. 22.6 per cent
  4. 78 per cent
Answer

Correct: A. It came in 1974’s exact manner: no good news, merely exhaustion plus the state’s printing press.

39The bezzle’s emblem surfaced in the panic’s deepest month when, on 11 December 2008:

  1. Lehman was liquidated
  2. AIG failed
  3. Bernard Madoff confessed that his celebrated fund — client statements totalling some 65 billion dollars — was a Ponzi scheme of decades’ standing
  4. The Fed announced QE
Answer

Correct: C. Undone not by any investigator but by the redemptions of frightened clients wanting their money in a crisis.

40Buffett’s verdict, saved by the volume for this chapter, is that:

  1. “You only find out who is swimming naked when the tide goes out”
  2. “Be fearful when others are greedy”
  3. “Price is what you pay, value is what you get”
  4. “Derivatives are weapons of mass destruction”
Answer

Correct: A. Warren Buffett, Berkshire Hathaway letter, 2001 — the bezzle liquidation dwarfed 2002’s.

41The crisis’s “bitterest residue”, the chapter says, is the asymmetry that:

  1. The banks were nationalised permanently
  2. Shareholders were fully protected
  3. No reforms were passed
  4. The mortgage machine’s frauds were settled by fines — well over 100 billion dollars, paid by shareholders — while senior US criminal accountability amounted, notoriously, to a single senior banker imprisoned
Answer

Correct: D. Public trust absorbed the difference — the political decade that followed collected it, with interest.

42Among the real structural reforms, Basel III:

  1. Forced capital multiples higher and invented liquidity ratios, so that thirty-times-levered, overnight-funded balance sheets became historical artefacts
  2. Abolished the banks
  3. Removed all capital requirements
  4. Applied only to hedge funds
Answer

Correct: A. Dodd–Frank pushed OTC derivatives into central clearing, and Britain ring-fenced retail banking from trading.

43The reforms’ honest scorecard, per the chapter, was never that risk was abolished but that:

  1. Risk was relocated — out of the newly armoured banks and into funds, shadow credit and the pension system’s hedging machinery
  2. Risk was eliminated from the system
  3. Risk was fully understood
  4. Risk had disappeared with the crisis
Answer

Correct: A. Leverage migrates to wherever it is least watched. It always has.

44The first recurring theme, “the crisis was assembled from safety”, holds that:

  1. Every component was recognised as dangerous
  2. AAA stamps, diversification mathematics, hedging insurance and the previous bust’s medicine were each sold as risk reduction — and the aggregation was the risk
  3. The crisis had a single cause
  4. Safety strategies always work
Answer

Correct: B. The same pattern as 1987’s portfolio insurance and LTCM’s convergence.

45“Incentives wrote the outcome” is captured by the maxim:

  1. “Buy low, sell high”
  2. “Show me the payoff and I will show you the conduct”
  3. “The trend is your friend”
  4. “Cut your losses”
Answer

Correct: B. Originate-to-distribute, issuer-paid ratings and IBG–YBG explain 2008 more completely than any villainy.

46“The run is the modern kind” means it was:

  1. Conducted by depositors at branches
  2. Prevented entirely
  3. Confined to a single country
  4. Conducted in repo and money markets by institutions — invisible until Northern Rock’s queues translated it
Answer

Correct: D. And stopped, as always, only by the balance sheet that cannot be run upon, on Bagehot’s terms.

47“The marks are a mechanism, not a record” identifies which unresolved tension?

  1. That the system’s honesty infrastructure was simultaneously indispensable and pro-cyclical — fair value transmitted and amplified the crisis through the doom loop
  2. That marking is always accurate
  3. That marks are irrelevant in a crisis
  4. That only regulators should set marks
Answer

Correct: A. A tension the volume presents as still unresolved.

48“The rescue purchased the next chapter” means that zero rates and QE:

  1. Restored the pre-crisis world exactly
  2. Had no lasting consequences
  3. Ended the emergency and began the era — the central bank as standing market participant, moral hazard institutionalised, and the bill deferred to the 2020s
  4. Prevented all future crises
Answer

Correct: C. The volume goes to the 2020s to collect the bill.

49The chapter describes the author’s vantage on the crisis as:

  1. A retail investor watching from home
  2. A mortgage broker
  3. A central-bank official
  4. The control side of a global investment bank — the daily, contested, career-staking negotiations over what a world of suddenly untradeable paper was worth
Answer

Correct: D. Which is to say, from the marks.

50The market’s behaviour after the March 2009 bottom — rising over 60 per cent inside the year while the headlines stayed apocalyptic — confirmed once more that the market is:

  1. A mirror of the economy
  2. The discounting machine, not the mirror, to the end
  3. Driven by good news
  4. Always late to recover
Answer

Correct: B. Prices moved on the printing press and exhaustion, not on any improvement in the headlines.

A register of sixteen figures behind the Great Financial Crisis — the officials who fought it, the bankers who built and broke it, the sceptics who profited and the Cassandras who warned, and the fraud the tide exposed. Two men named Paulson appear, on opposite sides of the story.

01

Ben Bernanke

American · b. 1953

Chairman of the Federal Reserve through the crisis, and the scholar uniquely equipped to fight it. Bernanke had spent his academic career studying the Great Depression and the credit channel, concluding that the central bank’s failure to act had turned a crash into a catastrophe — and in 2008 he applied the lesson, building the Bagehot machine of emergency facilities and launching quantitative easing, the money-printing lever this volume follows into the next chapter. Criticised for the moral hazard he institutionalised and for not seeing the storm sooner, he is nonetheless the architect of the rescue that stopped the run. His later Nobel recognised the very Depression scholarship the crisis let him deploy.

02

Hank Paulson

American · b. 1946

US Treasury Secretary through the crisis and, as a former chief executive of Goldman Sachs, a man who understood the instruments intimately. Paulson took Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac into conservatorship, wrestled the 700 billion dollar TARP through a hostile Congress after its first rejection crashed the market, and bears the contested judgement on letting Lehman fail — a decision he and his colleagues attributed to the absence of legal authority to lend into so deep an insolvency. Blunt and driven, he redirected TARP into bank equity on the British model when liquidity alone proved insufficient. He is the crisis’s chief fiscal firefighter — and not to be confused with the hedge-fund manager of the same surname below.

03

Timothy Geithner

American · b. 1961

President of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York during the crisis and, from 2009, Treasury Secretary — the official at the operational centre of the rescues. Geithner ran the weekend negotiations that sold Bear Stearns to JPMorgan and backstopped its book, was central to the AIG rescue, and later designed the bank stress tests that restored confidence by making the doom loop a supervised, public rehearsal. Pragmatic and controversial — accused of rescuing the banks while households foundered — he defended the choice as the least-bad path through a system on the edge of stopping. He completes the American troika, with Bernanke and Hank Paulson, that improvised the response in real time.

04

Mervyn King

British · b. 1948

Governor of the Bank of England through the crisis, and the author of its sharpest British caption — that it is not rational to start a bank run, but rational to participate in one once it has started. King’s early reluctance to provide liquidity, on moral-hazard grounds, was overtaken by the Northern Rock queues, the first run on a British bank since 1866; thereafter he supported the recapitalisation and quantitative easing that steadied the system. An academic economist by background, he later wrote with unusual candour about the crisis’s deep causes in the structure of money and banking itself. He personifies the tension this chapter dwells on: the lender of last resort’s dread of the hazard it must nonetheless underwrite.

05

Dick Fuld

American · b. 1946

The last chief executive of Lehman Brothers, whose bankruptcy on 15 September 2008 — the largest in history at some 600 billion dollars — falsified the market’s standing assumption that the system’s core would always be caught. Known on the Street as “the Gorilla” for his combative dominance, Fuld had built Lehman into a mortgage-securitisation powerhouse leveraged past thirty times and funded overnight, and refused, until too late, the capital and rescue terms that might have saved it. He remains the crisis’s most prominent casualty and its emblem of the leveraged broker-dealer model, extinct within a week of his firm’s collapse. No senior criminal charge ever attached to him.

06

Chuck Prince

American · b. 1950

Chief executive of Citigroup, then the largest bank in the world, and author of the crisis’s defining confession — that as long as the music was playing, one had to get up and dance, and Citigroup was still dancing. Delivered to the Financial Times in July 2007, weeks before the seizure, the remark was widely read as folly but is, this chapter argues, the beauty contest’s career mathematics stated by its most senior contestant: the risk officers could see the anatomy, but no institution could leave the floor while rivals danced. Prince resigned that November as Citigroup’s subprime losses mounted toward the largest of any bank, requiring repeated government support.

07

Fred Goodwin

British · b. 1958

Chief executive of the Royal Bank of Scotland, whose acquisitive ambition made his bank, briefly, one of the largest in the world — and then its most spectacular casualty. Goodwin drove the debt-fuelled 2007 purchase of ABN AMRO at the very top of the market, an acquisition that left RBS catastrophically short of capital when funding froze; by his own chairman’s later account the bank came within hours of being unable to fund, and was rescued into majority state ownership. Nicknamed “Fred the Shred” for his cost-cutting, he was later stripped of his knighthood — the British emblem of banking hubris, and of the truth that the reckoning fell unevenly.

08

Angelo Mozilo

American · 1938–2023

Co-founder and chief executive of Countrywide Financial, the largest US mortgage lender and the industrial heart of the originate-to-distribute machine this chapter dissects. Mozilo built Countrywide into a subprime powerhouse, writing the teaser-rate and low-documentation loans the securitisation pipeline demanded — volume over quality, because volume was what the pipeline paid for — before the firm collapsed into a distressed sale to Bank of America in 2008. He settled fraud and insider-dealing charges for a large sum without admitting wrongdoing, a resolution emblematic of the crisis’s asymmetry. He is the register’s figure for the supply side of the mortgage machine, where the alphabet’s raw material was manufactured.

09

Michael Burry

American · b. 1971

The physician turned investor who, reading mortgage prospectuses line by line from his small California fund, Scion Capital, became one of the first to grasp that the subprime alphabet was built to fail — and to act. Burry persuaded banks to create credit default swaps letting him bet against subprime mortgage bonds, absorbing years of premium payments and investor fury before the trade paid enormously as the pools collapsed. Solitary and singular, later revealed to have Asperger’s, he is the archetype of the analyst who reads what everyone can see and believes it — the counter to the euphoria in which, as the volume puts it, looking was the deviant act.

10

Steve Eisman

American · b. 1962

The outspoken hedge-fund manager who shorted the subprime machine from conviction as much as calculation, having concluded from close study of the lenders that the entire edifice was rotten. Running a fund within Morgan Stanley’s FrontPoint, Eisman built large positions against mortgage securities and the financial firms exposed to them, and became one of the crisis’s most quoted voices for his contempt of the incentives that drove it. Famously blunt, he treated the mortgage boom as a moral as well as an analytical failure. He is this chapter’s figure for the sceptic who was right on the fundamentals and, unlike the value managers of Chapter Six, positioned to be paid for it.

11

John Paulson

American · b. 1955

The hedge-fund manager who executed what became known as the greatest trade in history — and not to be confused with the Treasury Secretary above. Paulson identified the subprime bubble and, through credit default swaps on mortgage securities, positioned his funds to profit from its collapse, earning some fifteen billion dollars for the firm and several billion for himself in 2007 alone. His involvement in structuring some of the instruments he bet against later drew a landmark regulatory case against the bank that built them. Where Burry and Eisman are remembered for insight, Paulson is remembered for scale: the single largest wager on the crisis this chapter describes, and the largest payoff.

12

Nouriel Roubini

American · b. 1958

The economist who earned the nickname “Dr Doom” for warning, from 2006, that the US housing bubble would trigger a deep recession and a global financial crisis — a forecast dismissed at the time as alarmism and vindicated with brutal completeness. Roubini laid out, with unusual specificity, the chain from mortgage defaults through securitisation losses to a systemic banking crisis, and became one of the era’s most prominent public voices once events proved him right. Perennially bearish thereafter, he embodies this chapter’s hard lesson from Chapter Four — that being early is indistinguishable from being wrong until the moment, arriving late, when it is not.

13

Raghuram Rajan

Indian · b. 1963

The economist who, as chief economist of the International Monetary Fund, delivered the most prescient warning of the pre-crisis years — a 2005 paper asking whether financial development had made the world riskier, arguing that the incentives of bankers rewarded taking on tail risk that would prove catastrophic. Presented to an audience of central bankers honouring Alan Greenspan, it was received with open scepticism and even scorn; the crisis vindicated it precisely. Rajan later governed the Reserve Bank of India with distinction. He is the register’s Cassandra from within the establishment itself — the insider who named the incentive structure this chapter blames, and was dismissed for it.

14

Meredith Whitney

American

The banking analyst whose October 2007 report on Citigroup — arguing the bank was so undercapitalised it would have to cut its dividend — proved a turning point in the market’s recognition of the crisis. Whitney’s call, initially controversial and quickly vindicated when Citigroup did exactly as she predicted, made her one of the most influential voices on Wall Street and a symbol of the analyst willing to publish the unwelcome number. She stands, in this chapter, for the diagnostic honesty that the euphoria had rendered deviant: the readiness to look plainly at a balance sheet and report what it showed, against the consensus that looking away was safer.

15

Brooksley Born

American · b. 1940

The lawyer who, as chair of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission in the late 1990s, warned that the unregulated over-the-counter derivatives market posed a grave systemic danger and sought to bring it under oversight — and was overruled by the era’s most powerful economic officials, who feared regulation would drive the business offshore. The market she warned about, credit default swaps foremost, was precisely the instrument through which AIG’s failure threatened the system a decade later. Vindicated far too late, Born is this chapter’s reminder that the OTC opacity of Chapter One was a known danger, flagged in time by an official whose warning the consensus chose to bury.

16

Bernard Madoff

American · 1938–2021

The financier and former NASDAQ chairman whose confession, on 11 December 2008, that his celebrated fund was a Ponzi scheme of decades’ standing — client statements totalling some 65 billion dollars — supplied the crisis its emblematic fraud. Madoff’s scheme was undone not by any regulator, despite years of specific warnings ignored, but by the mechanism Galbraith described: the redemptions of frightened clients wanting their money in a crisis, which ended the inflows that had paid the fictitious returns. His exposure is the purest illustration of the bezzle this volume tracks — the boom supplies the inflows, the bust ends them, and only the tide’s retreat audits everyone at once. He died in prison in 2021.

Fifty questions drawn directly from the chapter’s content, in the order of its argument. Commit to a letter before expanding the answer.

1The final chapter describes itself as the biography of a regime created by the 2008 rescue — namely:

  1. A return to the gold standard
  2. Zero rates, standing QE, the central bank as permanent participant
  3. Free-market deregulation
  4. Fiscal austerity
Answer

Correct: B. The chapter collects the bills of that regime.

2From 2010, the crisis re-emerged in Europe as sovereign risk, with the doom loop in its purest form:

  1. Households defaulting on mortgages
  2. A currency peg collapse
  3. Peripheral banks stuffed with their own governments’ bonds, each side’s weakness marking down the other
  4. A stock-market crash
Answer

Correct: C. Spreads on Italian and Spanish debt priced redenomination itself.

3Draghi’s July 2012 sentence is called the most cost-effective intervention in central-banking history because:

  1. It cost nothing to announce
  2. Spreads collapsed on the words, and the backstop machinery announced behind them was never once drawn
  3. It was funded by other governments
  4. It replaced all other policy
Answer

Correct: B. In a confidence run, the credible promise of the infinite balance sheet is the intervention.

4February 2018’s “volmageddon” saw products built to short volatility exterminated in an afternoon — described as:

  1. A fraud
  2. 1987’s mechanism, miniaturised — the volatility created by their own crowding
  3. A currency crisis
  4. A central-bank error
Answer

Correct: B. One of the decade’s tremors rehearsing the same grammar in a minor key.

5By the decade’s end, the regime had produced conditions with no precedent, including the acronym:

  1. TINA — there is no alternative
  2. IBG–YBG
  3. ABX
  4. LIBOR–OIS
Answer

Correct: A. Policy rates at multi-century lows, and negative yields on well over fifteen trillion dollars of bonds at the extreme.

6The doctrine each of the decade’s tremors taught was:

  1. Sell into strength
  2. Avoid all leverage
  3. Cash is always king
  4. Dips are bought, because policy arrives
Answer

Correct: D. The pendulum of Chapter Three spent ten years being caught on every backswing.

7The pandemic crash fell how far, how fast?

  1. 50 per cent over two years
  2. 33.9 per cent in twenty-three trading sessions — the fastest fall of that depth ever recorded
  3. 22.6 per cent in a single day
  4. 10 per cent over a month
Answer

Correct: B. Faster than 1929, faster than 1987’s run-up-and-crash; the circuit breakers triggered four times in ten days.

8The “tell” that March 2020 was a Chapter One event and not merely a repricing was:

  1. Falling equity prices
  2. A wave of corporate frauds
  3. The dash for cash — days on which US Treasuries, gold and equities all fell together, and the deepest market on earth turned dysfunctional
  4. Rising inflation
Answer

Correct: C. When diversification fails inside the risk-free asset, the message is the volume’s oldest: in the storm there is only liquidity, and liquidity is cash already held.

9The chapter contrasts 2008 and 2020 as:

  1. Two identical credit crises
  2. Two frauds
  3. Two currency crises
  4. A slow strangulation (2008) and a trapdoor (2020) — a credit cycle turning versus the world’s cash flows being switched off by decree in a leveraged system
Answer

Correct: D. The mechanisms were entirely different in speed and cause.

10On the morning of 23 March 2020, the Federal Reserve announced:

  1. A rate rise
  2. QE without stated limit and — crossing a line intact since its founding — facilities to buy corporate credit
  3. The end of QE
  4. A bank nationalisation
Answer

Correct: B. Fiscal policy followed within days at wartime scale, with the CARES Act’s trillions and Britain’s furlough.

11The equity low of the entire pandemic crisis was printed:

  1. On 23 March 2020 — the date of the announcement, to the session
  2. Weeks after the Fed’s announcement
  3. In August 2020
  4. Before the pandemic began
Answer

Correct: A. The cleanest demonstration of the lesson repeated since 1987: in the policy era, the pivot is the bottom, and the bottom is a policy decision.

12Samuelson’s joke achieved its “final form” in 2020 because the market:

  1. Predicted the recession perfectly
  2. Failed to predict a recession that was actually happening, because it was pricing the response instead
  3. Ignored the pandemic
  4. Crashed and stayed down
Answer

Correct: B. The S&P 500 regained its February peak by 18 August 2020, a six-month round trip through the worst contraction since the 1930s.

13Dalio’s “cash is trash”, delivered at Davos weeks before the crash, was:

  1. Right immediately
  2. A market-timing call
  3. Never tested
  4. Spectacularly wrong for roughly five weeks, then spectacularly right twice over across the following two years
Answer

Correct: D. His actual claim was the long-cycle thesis — that in the late stage of a debt cycle, the currency itself becomes the release valve.

14The everything rally was Chapter Four performed at internet speed, with the euphoria act:

  1. Compressed from years into months
  2. Extended over decades
  3. Skipped entirely
  4. Replaced by distress
Answer

Correct: A. Liquidity without precedent met a locked-down population holding stimulus payments and zero-commission trading applications.

15January 2021’s emblem was GameStop, a declining retailer:

  1. With no short interest
  2. That went bankrupt
  3. With more shares sold short than existed, driven from under twenty dollars toward an intraday 483 by a networked crowd executing a short squeeze
  4. Acquired by a rival
Answer

Correct: C. A genuinely new social phenomenon wearing a perfectly ancient anatomy — and the retail wave was the marginal price-setter for the first time since the 1920s.

16The issuance flood of the era took the form of:

  1. Hundreds of SPACs — blank-cheque shells — floated in 2021 alone, supply expanding to meet the madness
  2. Government bonds
  3. Corporate loans
  4. Mortgage securities
Answer

Correct: A. Around it, crypto ascended to a 69,000-dollar bitcoin and the brief solemn valuation of joke currencies and JPEG receipts.

17Wirecard, Germany’s fintech champion and a DAX constituent, collapsed in June 2020 when:

  1. The €1.9 billion on its balance sheet was confirmed not to exist — its short-selling critics having been investigated by the authorities while the fraud ran on
  2. Its founder resigned
  3. A rival acquired it
  4. The pandemic closed its business
Answer

Correct: A. Enron’s lesson — that in euphoria looking is the deviant act — restaged with the regulator as enforcer of the not-looking.

18Archegos, in March 2021, detonated the charge planted in Chapter One because it was:

  1. A regulated bank
  2. A single family office running hidden, duplicated leverage through the total-return-swap books of half a dozen prime brokers — transmitting ten billion dollars of losses directly into the banking system
  3. A pension fund
  4. A cryptocurrency exchange
Answer

Correct: B. The largest single hit, 5.5 billion, landed on Credit Suisse — prime brokerage converting fund leverage into bank exposure, exactly to specification.

19The chapter observes that neither Wirecard nor Archegos slowed the party, because:

  1. They were kept secret
  2. They were too small to matter
  3. Distress signals, in the euphoria act, are entertainment
  4. Regulators concealed them
Answer

Correct: C. The tide itself turned only when the water was withdrawn — and the water, as ever, was the price of money.

20Through 2021 the official word for the stirring inflation was:

  1. “Permanent”
  2. “Imaginary”
  3. “Structural”
  4. “Transitory” — until the Federal Reserve’s chairman formally retired the word in November 2021
Answer

Correct: D. By mid-2022 US consumer inflation had reached 9.1 per cent, its highest in four decades.

21The policy response to the inflation was:

  1. A gradual, decade-long tightening
  2. No change at all
  3. Further rate cuts
  4. The fastest tightening cycle in forty years — Chapter Three’s gravity restored at escape velocity
Answer

Correct: D. Upon a financial system that had spent a decade being paid to assume it never would be.

22The “true signature” of 2022, distinguishing it from an ordinary equity bear market, was that:

  1. Only technology stocks fell
  2. The dollar collapsed
  3. Bonds crashed alongside equities — the US aggregate bond index losing around 13 per cent, its worst year on record, and long gilts over 40 per cent
  4. House prices doubled
Answer

Correct: C. Equity-crash losses inside the “safe” half of every portfolio.

23The classic 60/40 balanced fund suffered its worst year since the 1930s because:

  1. Stocks and bonds are always correlated
  2. The fund was poorly managed
  3. Inflation destroyed only bonds
  4. A decade of zero rates had made duration the common factor in everything — and when the discount rate rose, the diversification revealed itself as one trade wearing many labels
Answer

Correct: D. Stagflation-lite had removed the hiding places, precisely as 1974 taught.

24The crypto reckoning of 2022 culminated in November with FTX, revealed as:

  1. A well-run exchange
  2. A government project
  3. An 8 billion dollar hole, its founder later convicted of fraud and sentenced to twenty-five years
  4. A minor player
Answer

Correct: C. Galbraith’s bezzle, liquidated by redemption requests, in the Madoff manner, on the Madoff timetable.

25Defined-benefit pension schemes hedge their liabilities through liability-driven investment, which uses:

  1. Gilts held via repo and interest-rate swaps — leverage, in the service of prudence, adopted on regulatory encouragement
  2. Cash reserves only
  3. Equities alone
  4. Foreign currency
Answer

Correct: A. Genuinely sensible for each scheme alone — but carrying, in aggregate, a 1987 clause nobody had priced.

26The “1987 clause” hidden in the aggregated LDI system was that:

  1. Pensions would never be paid
  2. A sharp enough rise in yields triggers collateral calls; if schemes meet them by selling gilts, the selling raises yields further, triggering further calls
  3. Gilts would default
  4. Swaps would be banned
Answer

Correct: B. Portfolio insurance’s doomsday loop, rebuilt inside the pension system, pointed at the “risk-free” asset itself.

27The push that triggered the gilt crisis came on 23 September 2022 when a new administration announced:

  1. £45 billion of unfunded tax cuts into an already-inflationary economy
  2. An interest-rate rise
  3. A bank rescue
  4. Entry into the euro
Answer

Correct: A. The market repriced British sovereign risk over a weekend.

28Long gilt yields, which had never in modern memory moved 30 basis points in a day:

  1. Fell sharply
  2. Were suspended
  3. Stayed unchanged
  4. Rose by well over a full percentage point in three trading days, the thirty-year passing 5 per cent
Answer

Correct: D. The spiral engaged precisely as specified: collateral calls in hours, forced gilt sales, higher yields, further calls.

29The Bank of England’s 28 September statement — “temporary purchases… on whatever scale is necessary” — is described as:

  1. An admission of failure
  2. An ordinary open-market operation
  3. A rate rise in disguise
  4. The Draghi grammar, in English — the promise was the intervention
Answer

Correct: D. Against a facility of up to £65 billion, the Bank ultimately bought under £20 billion.

30The novel variation in the Bank’s gilt rescue was:

  1. A hard deadline of 14 October — a central bank imposing discipline while rescuing
  2. Buying equities instead of bonds
  3. Refusing to intervene
  4. Nationalising the pension schemes
Answer

Correct: A. The deadline held; the political structure that had supplied the push did not.

31The political consequence of the gilt crisis was that:

  1. The government was re-elected
  2. The Bank of England was abolished
  3. Nothing changed politically
  4. The Chancellor was dismissed on the 14th and the Prime Minister resigned on 20 October after forty-nine days — the shortest premiership in British history
Answer

Correct: D. The bond market’s verdict on unfunded arithmetic, delivered at ERM speed — a full circle to Chapter Six.

32The gilt crisis promoted which of the volume’s assertions “from thesis to case law”?

  1. That markets are efficient
  2. That central banks cause crises
  3. That diversification always works
  4. That leverage migrates to wherever it is least watched, and after 2008 it migrated into the safety machinery
Answer

Correct: D. Collateral buffers on LDI structures were raised several-fold as standing circuit breakers.

33The author’s visceral lesson from inside the gilt crisis was that:

  1. Gilts are always safe
  2. There is no such thing as a safe asset — there are only assets whose dangerous days have not yet been scheduled
  3. Pension schemes should not hedge
  4. The Bank acted too slowly
Answer

Correct: B. Every mechanism was familiar, except that the asset performing them was the one the entire edifice defines as riskless.

34Silicon Valley Bank failed in March 2023 because it had:

  1. Made bad property loans
  2. Parked a flood of technology-boom deposits in long-dated bonds — the gilt lesson in dollar form: duration risk in the safe asset
  3. Been defrauded by a trader
  4. Lent to cryptocurrency firms only
Answer

Correct: B. When rising rates sank those holdings, a capital raise spooked its concentrated, networked depositor base.

35The SVB run is described as “Northern Rock at fibre-optic speed” because:

  1. It saw 42 billion dollars of withdrawal requests in a single day, with a further hundred billion queued for the next morning — a run conducted by smartphone, no queues required
  2. It formed queues down the street
  3. It was a purely wholesale run
  4. It was contained without failure
Answer

Correct: A. The authorities invoked systemic exceptions to guarantee all deposits within the weekend.

36Days later the contagion crossed the Atlantic and took Credit Suisse, which was:

  1. Allowed to fail
  2. Nationalised permanently
  3. Merged into UBS over the weekend of 19 March at the Swiss state’s direction
  4. Rescued by the United States
Answer

Correct: C. Institutions, like markets, are ultimately confidence structures — and confidence, once spent, cannot be re-borrowed at any rate.

37The consequential technical footnote to the Credit Suisse rescue was that:

  1. Depositors lost their money
  2. Equity holders were wiped out first
  3. Some sixteen billion francs of AT1 bonds were written to zero while equity retained value — an inversion of the presumed hierarchy that repriced an entire asset class overnight
  4. No securities were affected
Answer

Correct: C. A reminder to read documentation before the weekend it matters.

38Dalio’s Changing World Order lens extends the debt machine across centuries, identifying a late-cycle syndrome in reserve-currency powers marked by:

  1. Debts too large to service honestly and increasingly monetised; widening internal wealth gaps expressing themselves as populist conflict; and the rise of an external rival
  2. Falling debt and social harmony
  3. Rising productivity alone
  4. A return to sound money
Answer

Correct: A. The Dutch, the British, the American — each rising on productivity and passing through the same arc.

39The volume’s position on the Dalio lens is that, as a locating device, it is:

  1. Worthless
  2. Relevant only to America
  3. A precise predictor of dates
  4. The best available — it predicted the shape of 2008 before 2008, and its late-cycle markers are facts, not opinions — but as a calendar it is nothing
Answer

Correct: D. Late stages run for decades; every assertion about timing applies to the biggest cycle most of all.

40The chapter’s summary table of five crashes shows that each policy response was:

  1. Smaller than the last
  2. Identical across crises
  3. Larger than the last, and each recovery faster — 21 months, then 17, then six — with each rescue’s cost carried forward into the next row’s mechanism
  4. Absent entirely
Answer

Correct: C. The table is Chapter Three’s ratchet, printed.

41The first closing theme, “policy is the other side of the trade”, holds that:

  1. Policy is irrelevant to markets
  2. Only fiscal policy matters
  3. Markets always move before policy
  4. The modern bottom is a policy decision — and analysis that models the market without modelling the state is doing half the arithmetic
Answer

Correct: D. From Draghi’s sentence to the 23 March 2020 pivot to the Bank’s gilt operation.

42“Leverage migrates to safety” means that, chased from the banks by reform, it re-emerged in:

  1. Government bonds alone
  2. Volatility products, family offices, technology deposits and the pension system’s own hedges — wherever the word “safe” had suspended the questions
  3. Retail brokerage accounts
  4. Foreign currencies
Answer

Correct: B. The dangerous asset is wherever “safe” has suspended the questions.

43“The anatomy now runs at internet speed, unchanged” is illustrated by:

  1. 2021 compressing Kindleberger’s five acts into eighteen months and SVB compressing the bank run into an afternoon — the software update being to the speed, never to the species
  2. A new kind of bubble never seen before
  3. The abolition of manias
  4. A slower pace of crisis
Answer

Correct: A. Not one element was new.

44“The rescue’s bill is inflation and moral hazard, collected next cycle” means that:

  1. 2020’s bazooka purchased 2021’s euphoria and 2022’s confiscation — the four levers of Chapter Three settling in sequence
  2. The rescue was free
  3. Inflation never returned
  4. Moral hazard was eliminated
Answer

Correct: A. Exactly as the framework prices them.

45The volume’s closing counsel, repeated one final time, is to:

  1. Predict the next crisis precisely
  2. Know where you stand, keep the liquidity that panic will price like oxygen, and remember that every chapter was written by people certain that theirs was different
  3. Stay fully invested at all times
  4. Trust the models above all
Answer

Correct: B. The bell, now as always, will not ring.

46The 2013 “taper tantrum” demonstrated that:

  1. QE could be ended painlessly
  2. Inflation was imminent
  3. The mere discussion of slowing QE convulsed bond markets and emerging economies
  4. Rates would never rise
Answer

Correct: C. One of the decade’s tremors rehearsing the regime’s grammar in a minor key.

47The chapter frames the whole QE decade’s lesson as a warning about what happens when:

  1. The catcher becomes the problem — after ten years of the pendulum being caught on every backswing
  2. Inflation stays low forever
  3. Markets are left entirely alone
  4. Regulation is abolished
Answer

Correct: A. The regime’s long levitation set up everything that followed.

48Two costs of the 2020 bazooka were “invoiced immediately and paid later” — the first being:

  1. A stock-market crash
  2. A currency collapse
  3. Moral hazard graduating — the put now covering corporate credit, so every future stress would be met by markets pricing the rescue before the damage
  4. A banking crisis
Answer

Correct: C. The second was the money creation into supply-constrained economies that loaded the inflation of Section V.

49The chapter reads “cash is trash” ultimately not as a market call but as:

  1. A joke
  2. The late-cycle thesis compressed — when the printing lever is the only one left, the currency absorbs the adjustment
  3. A prediction of March 2020
  4. Advice to avoid all assets
Answer

Correct: B. The same lens the chapter’s koan came from — measurement, not a calendar.

50The practitioner’s use of the Dalio lens, the chapter concludes, is Marks’s one final time:

  1. Predict the turning point exactly
  2. Ignore the cycle entirely
  3. Know where you stand; size and posture accordingly; predict nothing
  4. Trade aggressively at the extremes
Answer

Correct: C. Location, not prediction — the discipline the whole volume has argued for.

A register of thirteen figures behind the era the rescue built — the central bankers who fired the bazooka and fought the inflation, the impresarios and retail insurgents of the everything rally, the frauds the tide exposed, and the ministers whose arithmetic the bond market overruled in a fortnight.

01

Jerome Powell

American · b. 1953

Chairman of the Federal Reserve through the pandemic and the inflation that ended the era. On 23 March 2020 Powell fired the bazooka this chapter describes — quantitative easing without stated limit and, crossing a line intact since the Fed’s founding, facilities to buy corporate credit — printing the market bottom to the session. Then came the harder chapter of his tenure: having called the 2021 inflation “transitory”, he formally retired the word that November and pivoted to the fastest tightening cycle in forty years, restoring Chapter Three’s gravity at escape velocity. A lawyer by training rather than an economist, he is the presiding official of both the rescue and the reckoning — the man who institutionalised the put and then paid down its inflationary bill.

02

Andrew Bailey

British · b. 1959

Governor of the Bank of England during the gilt crisis, and the author of its decisive intervention. When the September 2022 mini-budget triggered the liability-driven-investment doom loop and long gilt yields gapped upward by the day, Bailey’s Bank declared a self-reinforcing spiral a material risk to financial stability and pledged, on 28 September, to buy long-dated gilts “on whatever scale is necessary” — the Draghi grammar in English, the promise doing most of the work. His genuinely novel variation was the hard 14 October deadline, a central bank imposing discipline even as it rescued. Having earlier run the Prudential Regulation Authority, he embodies this chapter’s lesson that the leverage reform chased out of the banks re-emerged inside the pension system he was obliged to save.

03

Rishi Sunak

British · b. 1980

Chancellor of the Exchequer through the pandemic and, from October 2022, the Prime Minister who restored market confidence after the gilt crisis. As Chancellor, Sunak designed the furlough scheme this chapter cites as Britain’s side of the wartime-scale fiscal response — the state underwriting incomes directly as the economy was switched off by decree. Succeeding Liz Truss in the immediate aftermath of the mini-budget, he and his Chancellor reversed the unfunded tax cuts and steadied gilt yields, the bond market’s confidence returning as quickly as it had fled. A former hedge-fund partner, he personifies the chapter’s closing circle — the fiscal firefighter of 2020 recalled to repair the fiscal fire of 2022.

04

Larry Summers

American · b. 1954

The economist and former US Treasury Secretary who, in early 2021, warned against the consensus that the era’s inflation would prove transitory — arguing that stimulus of unprecedented scale poured into a supply-constrained economy would ignite the fastest price rises in a generation. Dismissed at the time by the administration and much of the profession, Summers was vindicated with painful completeness as US inflation reached 9.1 per cent. He is this chapter’s Cassandra of the 2022 reckoning, playing the role Roubini played for 2008 and Rajan for the crisis before it: the credentialed insider who named the mechanism early and was told, until the invoice arrived, that this time the arithmetic did not apply.

05

Keith Gill

American

The retail investor, known online as “Roaring Kitty”, whose methodical case for GameStop galvanised the networked crowd that drove the stock from under twenty dollars toward an intraday 483 in January 2021. Gill’s posts — part value thesis, part rallying cry against the hedge funds shorting the shares — made him the emblem of a genuinely new phenomenon: a dispersed online community executing Chapter One’s short-squeeze mechanics against the professionals, and briefly becoming the marginal price-setter. Later called before Congress, he insisted he was simply an individual who liked the stock. He personifies this chapter’s observation that the software update was to the speed and the sociology, never to the ancient anatomy underneath.

06

Chamath Palihapitiya

Canadian–American · b. 1976

The venture capitalist and former Facebook executive who became the most visible impresario of the SPAC boom — the blank-cheque vehicles this chapter identifies as the era’s historic issuance flood. Launching a series of listed shells to take companies public, and promoting them with a showman’s flair to a retail audience, Palihapitiya was dubbed the “SPAC King” at the mania’s height, before many of the ventures floated on his enthusiasm fell heavily once rates rose. He stands for the supply side of the everything rally — the financier who, exactly as Chapter Four’s framework predicts, manufactured the instruments to meet the madness, issuance expanding to fill every ounce of the crowd’s appetite.

07

Cathie Wood

American · b. 1955

The founder of ARK Invest, whose disruptive-innovation funds became the emblem of the era’s growth euphoria. Wood built a devoted following with bold public forecasts for the technologies of the future and concentrated bets on profitless, high-growth stocks — the bespoke-metric investing this chapter places at the heart of the everything rally. Her flagship fund’s spectacular ascent through 2020 and steep reversal as rates rose in 2022 traced the boom-and-bust arc precisely: the same duration bet, unsigned, that undid every long-dated asset when the discount rate returned. She personifies the narrative machinery of Chapter Two — the compelling story of transformation that does the valuing when arithmetic has been suspended.

08

Markus Braun

Austrian · b. 1969

The chief executive of Wirecard, Germany’s fintech champion and a member of the DAX index, whose collapse in June 2020 opened the era’s bezzle. Under Braun, Wirecard reported some €1.9 billion of cash on its balance sheet that was ultimately confirmed not to exist — and, in the pattern this volume traces to Enron, the journalists and short-sellers who questioned the accounts were themselves investigated by the German authorities while the fraud ran on. His arrest and trial marked one of the largest accounting scandals in German corporate history. He is this chapter’s figure for the fraud that flourishes when rising markets suspend the looking and the regulator polices the sceptics rather than the books.

09

Sam Bankman-Fried

American · b. 1992

The founder of the cryptocurrency exchange FTX, whose implosion in November 2022 supplied the era’s emblematic fraud — an 8 billion dollar hole where customer funds should have been. Feted as a boy-genius philanthropist and courted by regulators and investors alike at the mania’s peak, Bankman-Fried presided over an exchange revealed, when redemption requests arrived in the downturn, to have channelled client money into his affiliated trading firm. Convicted of fraud and conspiracy and sentenced to twenty-five years, he is Galbraith’s bezzle liquidated on the Madoff timetable: the boom supplied the inflows that concealed the theft, and the bust ended the supply and exposed it.

10

Do Kwon

South Korean · b. 1991

The founder of Terraform Labs, whose algorithmic “stablecoin” TerraUSD and its paired token Luna collapsed in May 2022, erasing tens of billions of dollars and beginning the crypto ecosystem’s unravelling that FTX would complete. Kwon had promoted the design — a coin held at a dollar not by reserves but by a self-balancing mechanism with its sister token — with combative confidence, until a loss of confidence broke the mechanism and the two tokens spiralled to near-zero together. Pursued across jurisdictions and arrested, he faced charges on multiple continents. He personifies this chapter’s reminder that the era’s newest financial engineering rediscovered the oldest lesson: a promise of stability is only as good as the balance sheet behind it.

11

Liz Truss

British · b. 1975

The Prime Minister whose September 2022 mini-budget lit the fuse of the gilt crisis and whose premiership the bond market ended in forty-nine days — the shortest in British history. Truss and her Chancellor announced some £45 billion of unfunded tax cuts into an already-inflationary economy; the market repriced British sovereign risk over a weekend, long gilt yields gapped upward, and the leveraged pension-hedging system spiralled toward the crisis the Bank of England was forced to arrest. Her fall — the Chancellor dismissed, then her own resignation — is this chapter’s full circle to Chapter Six: the bond market’s verdict on unfunded arithmetic, delivered at the speed of Black Wednesday, on a government that had forgotten governments can lose.

12

Kwasi Kwarteng

British · b. 1975

The Chancellor of the Exchequer who delivered the mini-budget of 23 September 2022, and was dismissed twenty-three days later as its consequences unfolded. Kwarteng’s statement of unfunded tax cuts, made without the customary independent fiscal forecast, was the specific push that triggered the gilt spiral this chapter dissects — the collateral calls, forced sales and rising yields that threatened a substantial share of the nation’s retirement savings. A historian by training, he became the shortest-serving Chancellor of modern times save one who died in office, sacrificed on 14 October as the price of restoring confidence. He and Truss stand together as the ministers whose arithmetic the oldest bond market on earth overruled within a fortnight.

13

Greg Becker

American

The chief executive of Silicon Valley Bank, whose failure on 10 March 2023 was the rate shock’s first aftershock and the gilt crisis’s lesson in dollar form. Under Becker, the bank had parked a flood of technology-boom deposits in long-dated bonds — duration risk in the ostensibly safe asset — and when rising rates sank those holdings and a botched capital raise spooked its concentrated, networked depositors, the modern run arrived at modern speed: 42 billion dollars of withdrawals in a single day, conducted by smartphone. He is this chapter’s emblem of the run rebuilt for the digital age — Northern Rock at fibre-optic speed — and of the truth that duration risk in the “safe” asset is where the danger had quietly gone to hide.
